Preface
This project is the final Master’s thesis at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). The Master’s
thesis has taken place over the past five months in collaboration with DTU Construct and DTU
Sustain.
This project has allowed us to explore areas of statics and mathematics, presenting significant chal-
lenges but also offering a highly educational journey.
In the duration of this project, different digital tools have been used to assist with calculations,
simulations, and setup of written content. These include:
• Excel - for organising data, performing numerical calculations with Solver, and generating
graphs used throughout the project.
• Maple - for handling complex symbolic computations, graph generation, and numerical results.
• ChatGPT - for occasional grammar refinement and, when needed, assistance restructuring
sentences for clarity. All output from ChatGPT has been critically reviewed and verified by the
authors.
These tools have supported the workflow and improved the project and report in general.
Acknowledgements
This project would not have been possible without support. A big thank you to Per Goltermann
for thorough guidance and support throughout the project, as well as for many interesting academic
discussions and drawings on the blackboard. Also, a big thank you to Ida Maria Gieysztor Bertelsen
for great talks and guidance.
Thanks to Kasper V. Ommundsen and Frederik E.S. Hansen for supplying us with the datafiles,
from their project, that the numerical calculations are based on.
Lastly, a special thanks to Laust L. Lillebæk for creating a beautifully illustrated front and back
cover design.

Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
==PAGE:3==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
Abstract
A common loading scenario for walls involves both axial and lateral loads. For well-established ma-
terials such as concrete, material-specific standards have been developed, which simplifies the design
process. In contrast, lesser-known materials lack such standards, leading to uncertainties about their
behaviour. Developing fully analytical solutions for any given material would increase simplicity in the
design process and make it easier for new materials to gain acceptance in the construction industry.
This thesis aimed to evaluate to what extent second-order deflections and load-bearing capacities of
unfired clay walls could be determined using purely analytical methods, for a wall with combined
axial and lateral load. The investigation focused on two single walls with dimensions of 3 metres by
6 metres, constructed using unfired clay bricks (108 mm in depth) and subjected to various loading
scenarios. For simplification, the wall was assumed to behave as a homogeneous material and analysed
as a load-bearing element.
By deriving and implementing closed-form expressions, consistent theoretical models was investigated
to allow for quick estimation of the wall behaviour. Of three investigated models for the stress-strain
curve of the material, it was found that the linear stress-strain curve allows the most progress in
analytical calculations.
The polynomial stress-strain curve, despite requiring more computational
power, is much closer to experimental data for the stress-strain curves, thus yielding more precise
results. The computational limit for the second-degree polynomial stress-strain curve occurs when
deciding the second-order effects on a cracked cross-section.
A custom ”exponential” stress-strain
curve has been derived, however, calculations have proven more computationally demanding than is
reasonable to pursue further.
It is possible to analytically determine the cross-sectional capacity diagrams, for both cracked and
uncracked cross-sections, when the material strengths are the limiting parameters. It has been found
that to obtain the second-order moments, numerical calculations become necessary. For cracked cross-
sections the second-order moments and deflections can only be obtained for the simplest of stress-strain
curves.
The study considered both first- and second-order effects, where deflections and eccentricities were
investigated, leading to their corresponding second-order moments. Discrepancies came to light with
slender wall configurations, especially at lower E-moduli for the walls with pure lateral load. This is
where cracking became significant, and the analytical approach nonetheless remained a strong tool for
assessment of capacities and deflections.
To validate the theoretical models, finite element analysis was conducted using ABAQUS. This analysis
was done with a Concrete Damaged Plasticity (CDP) model calibrated with experimental data. The
comparison revealed that the FEM model and the theoretical model can approximate each other’s
behaviour adequately under high stiffness and moderate loads, particularly when second-order effects
are limited. However, significant divergence was observed in more slender configurations, at lower
E-moduli, with large second-order effects.
Allowing the wall to crack led to very large analytical
expressions which became impossible to evaluate based on the current mathematical programs used,
and the finite element program had diﬀiculty finding convergence when evaluating crack propagation.
During this project, the importance of material parameters was investigated to determine the future
focus of material optimisation.
The results led to the conclusion that the tensile strength has a
significant effect when considering walls with little to no normal force applied. For the walls with
larger second-order effects, it was seen that the elastic modulus had a major impact on whether the
wall would stay within capacity. The compressive strength seemed overall not to contribute much,
but if the assumption is maintained that the tensile strength is one-tenth of the compressive strength,
then increasing the compressive strength is equally important to tensile strength.
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls

1
Introduction
A common loading scenario for walls involves both axial and lateral loads. For well-established ma-
terials such as concrete, material-specific standards have been developed, which greatly simplify the
design process. In contrast, lesser-known materials lack such standards, leading to uncertainties about
their behaviour. Developing fully analytical solutions for any given material would increase simplicity
in the design process and make it easier for new materials to gain acceptance in the construction
industry.
This study will therefore explore the structural behaviour of unfired clay walls through static analysis
based on established structural principles. The investigation focuses on two single walls with dimen-
sions of 3 metres in height and 6 metres in width, constructed using unfired clay bricks (108 mm
in depth) and subjected to various loading scenarios. For simplification in the project, it has been
assumed that, the wall behaves as a homogeneous material.
The wall will be analysed as a load-bearing element within the interior layer of an external wall,
making it unaffected by external conditions such as rain and low temperatures.
This project will focus on investigating to what extent it is possible to determine capacities and
second-order effects, solely through the use of analytical calculations. Where analytical methods are
insuﬀicient, numerical methods will instead be employed.
To ensure realistic load conditions and
facilitate meaningful comparisons, all analytical solutions will be evaluated under a loading scenario
represented by a 4-storey building.
To understand how to properly enhance the performance of unfired clay, key material parameters
such as strength and stiffness will be examined. N-M diagrams will be used to assess the relationship
between normal force and moment while comparing these values to known material strengths.
The study will consider both first- and second-order effects, where deflections and eccentricities are
investigated, leading to their corresponding second-order moments.
The thesis will investigate the effect of allowing the wall to propagate cracks. To validate the theoretical
models, finite element analysis will be conducted. This analysis will use numerical models trying to
simulate the theoretical models as best as possible.
Finally, the parameters will be evaluated on the basis of the results to determine where the focus of
future parameter enhancement should lie.

Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749

2
Theory
2.1
Unfired Clay as a Material
Unfired clay is a natural building material made from clayey soil that has been shaped and dried
without undergoing high-temperature firing. Unlike traditional fired bricks, unfired clay is typically
used in forms such as adobe, rammed earth, or compressed earth blocks.
It is valued for its low
environmental impact and good thermal performance.
Unfired clay is a cohesive-frictional material that exhibits mechanical behaviour similar to materials
like concrete and soil.
Under compression, it displays quasi-brittle characteristics with non-linear
hardening up to peak stress followed by strain-softening and crushing, comparable to low-strength
unreinforced concrete. In tension, it has low capacity and brittle failure.
2.2
Deflections
To set up a theoretical model that can determine whether or not a wall has suﬀicient capacity to carry
the loads imposed on it, an N-M diagram for the cross-section of the wall is made. This diagram takes
the normal force and moment imposed, and transforms them into a point that shows how close to the
capacity the loads are. This requires knowing the value of the normal force and moment. The normal
force is simply just taken to be the maximum vertical load that acts through the wall. The moment
is the result of all the forces that cause a bending of the wall. This bending also causes the wall to
deflect, moving the centreline away from its initial position, relative to the normal force. This leads
to an increased, varying eccentricity to the normal force, as seen in Figure (2.1).
Figure 2.1: Deflection of central axis and eccentricity to normal force.
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
3
==PAGE:8==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
This eccentricity, combined with the normal force will create an additional moment throughout the
wall. With an additional moment, the deflection will increase accordingly. Naturally, an increased
deflection leads to an increased moment, and the cycle continues.
If statically feasible, this increase in deflection and moment will at some point start stabilising and stop
growing. The deflection at the stabilised point is known as the 2.-order deflection. The corresponding
moment is called the 2.-order moment.
A function, u(x), for the deflection along the height of the wall can be determined, if the corresponding
curvature function, κ(x), is known. The curvature function is the double derivative of the deflection
function. Transforming this into the deflection function being dependent on the curvature function
for a wall that starts at x = 0, equation (2.1) is written.
u(x) =
Z x
0
Z x
0
κ(x) dx dx + C1 · x + C2
(2.1)
Likewise, the function for the angle of deflection may be expressed as in equation (2.2).
θ(x) =
Z x
0
κ(x) dx + C1
(2.2)
The constant, C1 is the same for both equation (2.1) and (2.2).
2.3
General Cross-Section Analysis
The loads that are acting on the walls may be written as normal forces and moment actions. When
these forces are large enough, the wall will cease to be able to resist the load and failure will happen.
The material parameters that resist these actions and provide load capacity to the walls are the
compressive and tensile strengths.
These, however are stresses and can therefore not be directly
compared to the normal forces and moments. As such it is necessary to have some equations, that
correlate stresses to load actions.
Equations (2.3) and (2.4) make a relation between the stresses
throughout the cross-section and the internal normal forces and moments they lead to.
Nint =
Z
A
σ dA
(2.3)
Mint,x =
Z
A
z · σ dA
(2.4)
Nint is the total internal force that the cross-section is experiencing. Mint,x is the moment around the
x-axis, which acts along the plane of the cross-section and is perpendicular to the axis, z, which is also
along the cross-sectional plane.
σ is a function of the internal stresses distributed around the cross-section. It is well known that the
internal forces must be equal to the external ones. This means that equations (2.3) and (2.4) also
describe the the relation between the stresses in the cross-section and the normal forces and moments
acting on them.
The equations involve integrating the stress distribution over the entire cross-section. The length of
the wall is much greater than its thickness. Thus, it is assumed that the stresses are constant along the
longitudinal axis of the wall. This corresponds to analysing a wall of infinite length. Local deviations
that may occur near the ends of the wall are therefore neglected.

When calculating the effects on an infinitely long wall, the breadth is ignored which leads to the new
equations, (2.5) and (2.6).
N =
Z
t
σ dt
(2.5)
M =
Z
t
z · σ dt
(2.6)
where the z-axis acts along the thickness of the wall. The forces in the equations are the ones acting
on the wall and are defined as the normal force per length of the wall and the moment per length
of the wall. The origin point of the z-axis is placed at the centre of the cross-section, such that a
centrally placed normal force will not give rise to moment actions.
With this placement, the equations for normal force and moment are written as in equations (2.7) and
(2.8).
N =
Z
t
2
−t
2
σ(z) dz
(2.7)
M =
Z

2
−t
2
z · σ(z) dz
(2.8)
Not allowing the distribution of stresses in the cross-section to exceed the compressive and tensile
strengths, will set a limit on how large the internal forces can become, while still remaining within the
cross-sectional capacity. As such a capacity diagram of the cross-section, called an N-M diagram can
be made, where the axes are the normal force and the moment. The material strengths set limits to
how large the moment may be at a given normal force. This grants a circumference of loads that the
combination of normal forces and moments must remain within. Figure 2.2 shows the principle of an
N-M diagram for positive normal forces and moments.
Figure 2.2: Conceptual sketch of an N-M diagram, covering positive normal forces and moments.
Stress distributions are most often found in relation to strain, a stress-strain curve. The strain describes
the physical deformations of an element. When the deflections of an element, such as a wall, are not
significantly large, it is considered that plane cross-sections remain plane [1].
The linear strain function that describes this phenomenon in a cross-section is shown in equation (2.9).
ε(z) = ε0 + κ · z
(2.9)
Here, ε0 is the axial, central strain, caused by the normal forces acting on the cross-section. κ is the
curvature caused by bending from moment actions.

Figure 2.3 shows the relation between stresses and strains through a cross-section.
Figure 2.3: Relation between strains and stresses throughout a cross-section.
When one is positive/negative the other is accordingly positive/negative. When there is no strain,
the cross-section is at rest, which means, there is no stress. When the stress is as high as it can be,
dictated by the compressive strength, the strain is at its maximum. When the stress reaches its tensile
strength, the strain is at its minimum.
Thus the stress state throughout the cross-section can be determined through the linear strain distri-
bution.
There exists many different types of stress-strain curves, since they are material dependant, and at
times different functions are used for compression and tension. As such it may be necessary to split
up the stress-strain functions as in equation (2.10).
σ(ε) =
 σt(ε)
if ε ≤0
σc(ε)
if ε > 0
(2.10)
Where σt(ε) is the tensile part of the stress-strain curve and σ(ε) is the compressive part.
This means that if there is both tension and compression in the cross-section, equations (2.7) and
(2.8) will be rewritten to equations (2.11) and (2.12).

z0 = −ε0

If there is no tension in the cross-section, equations (2.7) and (2.8) are rewritten to equations (2.14)

Since the strengths of the material dictate the capacity of the cross-section, there are specific cases,
of stress that will be critical for the cross-section. These stress cases are shown in Figure 2.4.
Figure 2.4: Critical stress cases for cross-section capacity.
The first critical stress-case, Case 1, is when the stresses have reached the tensile strength at one end
of the cross-section. If the stresses are any lower, they will cause the cross-section to crack, reducing
its effective thickness. Case 2 occurs, when the stresses have reached the compressive strength in
one side of the cross-section and while there is still some tension in the other side. Case 3 occurs,
when there is no tension in the cross-section and at least one side experiences stress equal to the full
compressive strength. If the stress in cases 2 or 3 increases, there will be crushing in the side of the
cross-section, where the compressive strength is exceeded.
Since this report is only concerned with positive normal forces, there is no case, where there is full
tension through the cross-section. Since the cross-section is perfectly symmetrical around its central
axis, the N-M diagram will be mirrored around the N-axis. This means that the resulting moment
capacity is numerically the same positive as negative. As such, only calculations are made based on
positive moments. If the moment for any given load point is negative, its absolute value should be
taken and it will fit the N-M diagram correctly.
Due to the relation between stresses and strains, it is possible to set up equations for the curvatures,
that apply for these cases. If the moment is positive, the point where the tension is highest is at
z = −t
2. Conversely, the point of the largest compression is at z = t
2. The cases are fulfilled, when the
strains reach their minimum and maximum, respectively, at these points. Thus equation (2.9) can be
transformed into equations (2.16) and (2.17) to grant the curvatures that satisfy these cases.
κt = −2
t · (εmin −ε0)
(2.16)
κc = 2
t · (εmax −ε0)
(2.17)
Here, κt satisfies case 1 and κc satisfies cases 2 and 3.

When the normal force is low, the compression is small, which means that case 1 will be dominating.
If the stress at one side is kept at the tensile strength and the normal force is increased, at some point
stress equal to the compressive strength is reached at the opposite side. At this point, both case 1 and
2 is reached as κt becomes equal to κc. The central strain, at which this switch from case 1 to case 2
occurs, is determined by setting equation (2.16) equal to equation (2.17). This central strain is given
by equation (2.18).
ε0 = εmin + εmax
2
(2.18)
Increasing the normal force requires reducing the curvature such that the tension is reduced. At some
point, there will be no tension in the cross-section. This is where z0 = −t
2. The curvature from
equation (2.17) can be inserted in place of the curvature in equation (2.13) to get the position of zero
strain for cases 2 and 3. Setting this position equal to −t
2 and solving for the central strain, results

2.4
Stress-Strain Curve
A stress-strain curve describes the material’s response under uniaxial loading. It characterises how
stress develops as a function of strain and can reflect both elastic and inelastic behaviours. The curve
typically starts with a near linear-elastic region, followed by a non-linear section that represents plas-
ticity, cracking, hardening or softening, depending on the material. The difference between a linear
and a non-linear stress-strain curve can be seen visualised in Figure 2.5.
Figure 2.5: Representation of a linear and non-linear stress-strain curve.
This function is essential to define how stress is distributed across a cross-section under the combination
of axial and bending loads, when combined with the strain distribution function in equation (2.9).
8
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
==PAGE:13==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
2.5
ABAQUS - Finite Element Modelling
ABAQUS is a finite element software used to evaluate stress distributions on an element, which helps to
assess general stress levels and identify potential failure points. It analyses deformations and deflection
patterns to understand how an element responds under loading.
When using ABAQUS, whether for linear or non-linear analysis, the stress-strain relationship needs
to be defined under the Material section of the model input. To match the theoretical model, three
material model instances are investigated: Linear, Non-linear, and Non-linear with cracks/damage.
Material Models in ABAQUS
Linear-Elastic Material Behaviour:
In linear-elastic analyses, a fully elastic material response is assumed, meaning the element will return
to its original shape after the load is removed. The ”Elastic” model in ABAQUS can then be used to
simulate this behaviour. To use this model, certain properties need to be specified, which include:
• Elastic modulus (E): Describes the material stiffness.
• Poisson’s ratio (ν): Describes the ratio of lateral strain to axial strain.
The E-modulus is derived from experimental stress-strain data by finding the initial slope of the curve.
Concrete Damage Plasticity (CDP):
For non-linear analyses, the material exhibits plastic behaviour, meaning it undergoes permanent
deformation after the elastic limit is reached. To define this, a specific material model must be chosen.
ABAQUS offers a wide selection of plasticity models for different purposes. To simulate non-linear
behaviour, in this project, the Concrete Damaged Plasticity (CDP) model is chosen.
CDP
consist of a initial elastic region followed by plastic behaviour. CDP is primarily used to evaluate the
post-yield behaviour of concrete in both tension and compression, making it highly suitable for the
investigative purposes of this project. CDP can be used both with and without damage factors which
can simulate many purposes.
CDP without damage:
To simulate non-linear behaviour without damage, damage factors are omitted. One assumption for
the theory is that the tensile behaviour can follow the theory of a reinforced material as long as no
damage is assumed and is in linear-elastic region (no activation of reinforcement). The reinforced
theory looks on the relation between tensile stress and cracking strain. This leads to the following
overview of the necessary inputs when calculating undamaged, shown in Table 2.1.
Table 2.1: Concrete Damage Plasticity (Undamaged) Required Input [2]
Parameter

Note
Elastic modulus
E0
Influences the stiffness

Account for lateral strains

==PAGE:14==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
The E-modulus and the Poisson’s ratio are the same parameters as in the linear-elastic model. The
five parameters in the middle in Table 2.1 has a specific influence on how the material behaves under
load in the simulation. The dilation angle, ψ, controls how much volume the material tends to expand
under shear. A higher angle implies more lateral expansion, which can affect crack propagation and
convergence. The eccentricity, e, Influences the shape of the plastic potential surface; lower values
make flow more shear-dominated. The compression ratio, (fb/fc), determines the shape of the yield
surface under multiaxial stress states. The shape parameter, (Kc), adjusts the balance between tensile
and compressive failure. The viscosity parameter is not a direct material behaviour, but can be used to
add a small damping effect to the model to aid with convergence during sudden softening or cracking,
especially under rapidly changing loads or highly brittle responses.
To define the stress-strain behaviours in ABAQUS, knowledge of the stress-strain curve, derived from
experimental data, needs to be provided. However, experimental tests yield engineering stress and
strain values, which must be converted to true stress and true strain before being input into ABAQUS.
Both compression and tensions follow the same theory of constant volume and can therefore both use
the following conversion formulas:
σtrue = σeng · (1 + εeng)
(2.21)
εtrue = ln(1 + εeng)
(2.22)
Equations (2.21) and (2.22) account for the continuous change in the specimens cross-sectional area
and length during deformation, and are therefore necessary for ABAQUS to accurately represent
material behaviour in the plastic range. However, this conversion is only valid up to around peak
stress, which is where deformation stop being uniform. Beyond the peak, cracking or crushing leads to
localized deformation, and the response no longer only reflects material behaviour but rather element
behaviour. Therefore, post-peak softening should be treated differently, typically by using fracture
energy or damage-based approaches. The conversion from engineering to true stress-strain is essential,
as ABAQUS interprets input data as true values. Supplying unconverted engineering data will result
in inaccurate simulations, particularly in the plastic range.
To investigate the permanent deformation in a undamaged instance, the inelastic strain needs to be
determined. The inelastic strain is determined as follows in equation (2.23).
εin = εtrue −σtrue
E0
(2.23)
Here, εin is the inelastic strain required as ABAQUS input. In the absence of damage, this is directly
equivalent to the plastic strain. The plastic strain is the permanent deformation of an element that
remains even after the stress, which has caused the deformation, is removed.

The material can still yield and undergo hardening and softening, but the unloading stiffness remains
equal to the initial elastic modulus E0 as seen on Figure 2.6 and Figure 2.7 on the line without damage
factor respectively, dc and dt.
Figure 2.6: Compression behaviour in uniaxial CDP model. [2]

CDP with damage:
Furthermore, it is investigated when the material is allowed to crack. This is possible by using Concrete
Damage Plasticity with damage parameters included.
The damage parameters affect both the stress calculation and unloading path in the elastic region [2].
This is also seen from Figures 2.6 and 2.7. The relation between stresses and strain becomes:
σ = (1 −d)E0ε
(2.24)
The damage variables are tension-specific (dt) and compression-specific (dc). The damage factor is
typically based on loading and unloading cycles caused by the change in modulus of elasticity[3]:
d = 1 −Ei
E0
(2.25)
If no loading/unloading data is available, the damage parameter can be approximated as follows:
d = 1 −σtrue
σtrue,u
(2.26)
When looking at the visual representation of damage on Figure 2.6, it shows the general principals
of a compressive behaviour and its effect on unloading. In the figure it is seen that the the elastic
strain describes the initial strain until yield point σc0 and the inelastic strain describing the post yield
strain. The damage will lead to reduction in stiffness when unloading.
Once damage is considered, the assumption that the material behaves similarly to reinforced concrete
without cracking no longer holds. For concrete-like materials, such as unfired clay, crack propagation
is commonly assumed to be governed by fracture energy. The fracture energy criterion reflects the
necessary energy to open a crack which will propagate until the stress in the point tip of the crack
reaches zero. The fracture criterion used is the post-failure stress-fracture energy criterion, which can
be seen on Figure 2.8. This fracture criterion investigates the propagation in terms of the relation
between stress and cracking deflection, as opposed to the cracking strain when reinforced.
Figure 2.8: Fracture energy curve. [2]
Figure 2.8 highlights the relation between cracking deflection and tensile stress. The area underneath
the graph is equal to the fracture energy. The fracture energy is denoted as the energy required to
open a crack of a unit of area. [4]
12
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
==PAGE:17==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
ABAQUS has a feature that allows for input of a fracture energy along with tensile strength and it
will compute the linear behaviour seen on Figure 2.8 it self. The cracking deflection is needed for the
damage factor. The cracking deflection is determined by:
ucr = 2 · Gf
σt,u
(2.27)
Where Gf denotes the fracture energy in N/m and σt,u denotes the tensile strength.

3
Results and Analysis
3.1
Loads
The loads calculations in this project will be overestimated by using quicker calculations and assump-
tions to simplify since the focus of the project is on the statics and not the calculation of loads.
3.1.1
Loading Scenario
To test the analytical equations and assist in solving numerical problems, a load case scenario is
established. This scenario is depicted in Figure 3.1, which illustrates the structural layout of a 4-storey
building. The walls in red are investigated in this project.
Figure 3.1: Building plan and sectional views
The dimensions of a single wall is 6 meters in length, 3 meters in height, and 0.108 meters in thickness,
corresponding to the standard width of a Danish brick. [5]
The purpose of this setup is to analyse the structural behaviour of two specific walls under varying
loading conditions: one located on the ground floor and the other on the 3rd floor. The ground floor
wall is of particular interest as it carries the largest vertical load due to the cumulative weight from
the upper stories, making it a critical element in the overall load-bearing system. Additionally, this
wall is subjected to a wind load, which will be investigated.
In contrast, the wall on the 3rd floor experiences the lowest vertical load, but it is subjected to the
largest wind pressure due to its exposed position. This makes it an interesting case for evaluating the
influence of horizontal loads under minimal axial stress.
Each of these two walls will be analysed under an assumed critical load combination. These loads
are not the result of detailed structural calculations, but are intended to represent quick, conservative
estimates that meet or exceed typical critical load levels for such scenarios.
14
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
==PAGE:19==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
The applied loads include:
• Live load from the floors, representing occupants, furniture, and equipment. A value of 2.5
kN/m2 is assumed, consistent with Eurocode 1 for oﬀice buildings (Category B). [5]
• Self-weight of structural components, including the walls, floors, and roof, calculated based
on material densities and geometric dimensions.
• Wind load, applied as both suction and pressure acting horizontally on the walls and vertically
on the roof, according to Eurocode 1. [6]
• Snow load, applied vertically on the roof, also following Eurocode 1 guidelines. [7]
The following necessary assumptions have been made:
• The wind load has been determined for the wall on the 3rd floor and it is assumed that it is
constant over the whole facade, so the walls below experience the same force. This is a slight
overestimation, but makes the calculations simpler and the situation more critical.
• The self-weight of a wall has, for sake of simplicity, been placed on top of the wall as a single
force. This means, the whole wall experiences the total load of the wall, rather than just the
bottom experiencing it. The density of the wall has been assumed to be 2100 kg/m3, which is
in the normal range for unfired clay.
• When transferring loads down the building, eccentricities must be accounted for. An initial
eccentricity of 5 mm (e0) has been assumed to represent the imperfections from construction.
The load from each slab onto the corresponding wall is assumed to act at a point located at
one-sixth of the wall thickness from the centreline (i.e., an eccentricity of t/6). This t/6 comes
from a triangular distributed force which results in a resultant force t/3 from the inside edge of
the wall, which is t/6 from centreline of the cross-section.
• It is necessary to make assumptions for the structural support system. Each wall is modelled
with a fixed base and a simply supported condition at the top, as illustrated in Figure 3.2, which
aims to replicate realistic boundary conditions. As the slabs have been assumed to be simply
supported, at both ends, by the load-bearing walls, they result in an equal distribution of loads
to each supporting wall, See Figure 3.2, right side.
Figure 3.2: Assumed support conditions: (Fixed-to-simple) and (Simple-to-simple).
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
15
==PAGE:20==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
Figure 3.3 shows the acting loads on the wall. The left diagram displays the separation of vertical
loads: A central line load Nc from the above construction and an eccentric line load Ne from the
slab on the given floor. The eccentricity of the load from the slab is made up of a constructional
imperfection e0 and a geometric contribution t/6, resulting in:
e1 = t
6 + e0
Both vertical forces act in the presence of a uniformly distributed horizontal wind load qwind.
In the right diagram on Figure 3.3, the two vertical line loads are combined into a resulting total normal
force, N, acting with an effective eccentricity eeff. This simplification enables direct assessment of
the moment induced in the walls cross-section and results in shorter analytical expressions along with
easier modelling of the Finite Element model.
Figure 3.3: Acting load scheme.
As the wind may come from multiple directions, the wind load may act either in compression, where
the load becomes positive, or as suction, where the load becomes negative. When the wind load is
positive, it will attempt to push the wall into the building. When the wind load is negative, it will
attempt to pull the wall away from the building. The eccentric normal force will lead to moment,
where, if the total eccentricity is positive, the wall will bend out and away from the building. When
the total eccentricity is negative, it will cause the wall to bend into the building.
16
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
==PAGE:21==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
The wind load and the eccentric normal force may either act with each other, where they bend the
same direction, or against each other, where they will attempt to cancel out the bending of the other
load. A visual representation of these two situations is shown in Figure 3.4.
Figure 3.4: Modes of bending for the wall. left: loads acting opposite each other. right: loads acting
with each other.
When the moments from the loads act against each other, the overall maximum moment is reduced.
The deflection is also reduced, which will lead to lower 2.-order deflections and subsequently, lower
2.-order moments.
As such, the most critical behaviour for the loads occurs when their bending
actions turn in the same direction. When performing capacity validating calculations, only the critical
situation needs investigating. Therefore, in this report, only the situations in which the wind load and
eccentric normal force act together are investigated.
When calculating the critical load combinations the following results are gotten as seen in Table 3.1.
Detailed calculations can be found in Appendix [A.1].

3.2
Stress-Strain Curves
As is evident from equations (2.7) and(2.8), the stress-strain curve, especially the shape thereof, may
prove important, when determining the internal normal force and moment. In this chapter, the goal
is to set up functions that describe the stress-strain curve of any given wall of unfired clay bricks.
To aid in finding the expression that allows the best fit to the material behaviour, data collected for
a project made by Kasper V. Ommundsen and Frederik E.S. Hansen [8] has been provided.
This data is the result of compression tests performed sample walls, consisting of three unfired moraine
clay bricks, produced by Egen Vinding & Datter, that have been placed on top of each other on their
bed faces. Between the bricks, a thin 3 mm layer of clay mortar was applied. These wall samples were
made small and as such prevent any effect of instability from influencing the results to any discernible
degree. One of such wall samples are shown in Figure 3.5.
Figure 3.5: 3-brick wall sample after testing. [8]
Five of such sample walls have been tested. The data from the experiments consists of the force
applied on the specimens along with the absolute deflection.
These have been transformed into
stresses and strains. The unfiltered data, for the five tests, with stresses and corresponding strains are
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
19
==PAGE:24==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
shown in Figure 3.6a. After transforming the force-displacement curves into stress-strain curves, some
assumptions are made for the curves. From the experimental data there is a long initial period where
the curve is slowly gradually increased until a linear zone is reached. This initial flat part is likely
due to a lack of proper contact between the machine and the test specimen. The data is therefore
cut to start when the initial linear behaviour starts. Now, if the linear slope is followed down to zero
stress, it will not reach zero strain. Another adjustment is then made to shift the strain positions of
the whole dataset such that the initial slope passes through the (0,0)-point. This is done since there
cannot be any deformation without any stresses, and vice versa. Finally, the data points after the top
points of each curve are removed, as theyserve no purpose for the investigation. The final, transformed
dataset for the experimental stress-strain curves are shown in Figure 3.6b. One of the curves deviates
significantly from the others, but nothing in the report from Ommundsen & Hansen marks this as an
error, so it is included here.
(a) Initial (Unfiltered)
(b) Transformed

June, 2025
In this report, three different functions, to represent the stress-strain relationship, are investigated.
These are a linear function, a 2.degree polynomial, and an ”exponential” function. These three models
are presented in equations (3.4) - (3.6)
Linear:
σ(ε) = a · ε + b
(3.4)
Polynomial:
σ(ε) = a · ε2 + b · ε + c
(3.5)
”Exponential”:
σ(ε) =
a
1 + eb·(ε+c) + d
(3.6)
Important to note, is that the constants of the 3 functions are not the same (a for linear isn’t equal
to a for the polynomial and so on).
The linear model represents the simplest case and is versatile in calculations, due to its simplicity. It
does, however deviate fast, when the data points start flattening out.
The 2.degree polynomial bends fits much closer to the data points, without sacrificing much in terms
of ease of calculation.
The ”exponential” function is custom designed and is not a regular exponential function, hence the
quotation marks. This function is designed to be versatile in regards to fitting with the data. It forms
an S-shape with a linear middle part, suitable for both untransformed and transformed experimental
data. It is also possible to set the initial slope and how sharply the curve flattens out. It does, however
come at the cost of a more complicated expression to calculate.
There are a few boundaries that the three models will follow. As with the dataset, it is important,
that when the material experiences no strain, there should similarly be no stress. The other boundary
follows that stress-strain curves often behave linear-elastic at its initial section. The initial slope is
known as the initial Elastic modulus. This is a common parameter when working with materials. As
such, the three models must be dependent on this value. The general conditions for the three models
are shown in equation (3.7)
σ(0) = 0
∧
E0 = dσ(ε)
dε

ε=0
(3.7)
Furthermore, specific conditions are applied for the polynomial and ”exponential” stress-strain curves.
The polynomial stress-strain curve has a top point. The height of this point is set to be at same
value as the compressive strength, fc, as this is also where the experimental data starts reducing in
compression.
The ”Exponential” stress-strain curve does not have a top point. However, it does have an S-shape.
This means that it may increase gradually, before reaching the inflection point. At this inflection point
the function behaves linearly, i.e. the double derivative is zero. As the experimental stress-strain curves
are set to have a linear start, this point should be at zero strain.
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
21
==PAGE:26==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025

models are determined and shown in equations (3.8) - (3.10).
Linear:
a = E0
∧
b = 0
(3.8)
Polynomial:
a = −E02
4 · fc
∧

∧
c = 0
(3.9)
”Exponential”:
b = −4 · E0
a
∧

∧
d = −a
2
(3.10)
Here, only the constant, a, for the ”exponential” stress-strain curve remains unknown. This value
must be found numerically by setting it such, that the function has the best fit with the data.
Putting these constants into their respective stress-strain functions, the expressions for the compres-
sive stresses of the stress-strain models become as described in equations (3.11) - (3.13).
Linear:
σc(ε) = E0 · ε
(3.11)
Polynomial:

3.3
2.-Order Deflections (Uncracked)
The purpose of this chapter is to find the maximum 2.-order moment, such that it can be compared
to the capacities in the N-M diagrams. To achieve this, it is required to know the maximum deflection
with the 2.-order effects.
The normal force and moment of equations (2.7) and (2.8) are intrinsically dictated by the central
strain and curvature. Conversely, if the external normal force and moment are considered known, it is
possible to use these equations to isolate expressions for the central strain and curvature. Especially
a function for the curvature is important as it may be used in equation (2.1) to grant a deflection
function that further will grant the maximum deflection required for the maximum 2.-order moment.
The supports of the wall leads to three boundary conditions for the deflections. The simple support at
the top restrains any deflections along the z-axis. The fixed support at the bottom further restrains
any movement along any axis. The fixed support also demands that the angle of deflection must be
zero at the support. Equation (3.16) sums up these boundary conditions.
u(0) = 0,
u(L) = 0,
θ(0) = du(x)
dx

x=0
= 0
(3.16)
This leaves three boundary conditions to solve the two unknown constants in equation (2.1). By their
nature, equations (2.1) and (2.2) will always have the same value at x = 0, regardless of the curvature
function. These are shown in equation (3.17)
u(0) = C2,
θ(0) = C1
(3.17)
This knowledge makes it possible to determine a general solution for the constants C1 and C2. com-
paring equations (3.16) and (3.17), the constants are found to be as in equation (3.18).
C1 = 0,
C2 = 0
(3.18)
To aid in finding an analytical expression and to make calculations easier, an overestimating assump-
tion is made. This assumption states that rather than the deflection varying throughout the height of
the wall, causing varying moment effects, the moment effect is simplified. The maximum deflection
is taken to be the constant deflection throughout the entire height of the wall, leading to a constant
moment from the deflecting eccentricity and normal force. This is equivalent to simply placing the
moment at the top of the wall. This will add another eccentric term to equation (3.1), leading to the
new equation (3.19) for the eccentric normal force.
M1(x) = N · (eeff −umax) ·

Since the deflection is determined as the movement of the centre along the z-axis compared to its
original position, and not the displacement of the normal force compared to the centre, the added
term is negative.

of the wall, may be written. This function is seen in equation (3.20).

The cross-section of the wall is rectangular, which means that the N-M diagram is mirrored above and
below the N-axis. As such, knowing the absolute value of the maximum 2.-order moment is all that is
required in the check for capacity exceedance. The largest moment will either be found at the bottom
of the wall, at the top, or at the extreme point. Equation (3.23) grants a function for the absolute

3.3.1
General Procedure for Finding Maximum Deflection and Moment
A general procedure for the intended method of determining the maximum 2.-order moment is written.
This procedure should be common for all different types of stress-strain curves.
1. For the given stress-strain curve, calculate equations (2.7) and (2.8) for the normal force and
moment.
2. Next, the expressions for the central strain and curvature are isolated in the calculated expres-
sions from step 1.
3. Now the function for the moment distribution along the height of the wall is inserted in place
of the moment in the expression for the curvature.
4. Then the curvature function is integrated as in equation (2.1), considering the constants in
equation (3.18). If an unknown constant from the curvature equation remains, the last condition
in equation (3.16) is used.
5. Taking the first derivative of the deflection function results in the angular function, θ(x). setting
this function to zero and solving for x grants the points, where the deflection reaches an extreme
point.
6. This position, dubbed xext, is then inserted into the deflection function u(x), thus granting
equation (3.24).
umax = u(xext)
(3.24)
If the deflection function includes a term with umax, which it will if it contains the moment
distribution, M(x), it is transformed so that the only umax is at the left-hand side of the equation.
7. When umax has been determined, it is put into the moment distribution function, M(x).
8. Now the extreme moment position is found as per equation (3.22). Lastly, the maximum 2.-order
moment is found through using equation (3.23).

summation. Equation (3.55) shows how an integral may be approximated using sums.

3.3.5
”Exponential” Stress-Strain Curve
The deflection and moment calculations are now performed on the ”exponential” stress-strain curve
that behaves linearly in tension.
Using equations (2.11) and (2.12) on this stress-strain curve grants the normal force and moment
equations (F.1) and (F.2). These equations are too large to be put in the report, so they are found in
Appendix [F].
Equation (F.2) contains a special type of function called polylog. Polylog is a non-analytical expression,
its behaviour described in equation (3.62):
polylog(a, z) =
∞
X
i=1
zi
ia
(3.62)
Both equations include a term with curvature. As such, the equations (F.1) and (F.2) should be solved
with respect to the central strain and curvature, respectively. However, it has not been possible to
compute a solution for these variables. This means, that to obtain any type of solution, numerical
methods should be taken in use. Though possible to do, it is not computationally feasible to pursue
the second-order moment for the ”exponential” stress-strain curve.
Making a purely ”exponential” stress-strain curve as with the polynomial case, will yield the exact
same problem as now. Calculations of this may be found in Appendix [C.8].
As a result of this, there are no calculations or results for the load points based on the ”exponential”
stress-strain curve.

of 590.6 MPa, given by Table 3.6 for the material parameter of the walls, the deviation is at 10.5%.
This is reduced to 5.1% at 700 MPa.

At some point, the E-modulus may become so low, that instability occurs.
Beyond the point of
instability, the added deflection from 2.-order effects will not reach an equilibrium, but will instead
increase indefinitely. The E-moduli threshold for instability, along with the corresponding maximum
deflections and moments, are shown in Table 3.10.

E-modulus cannot be reduced more.

3.4
Cross-Section Capacity Analysis (Uncracked)
3.4.1
General Procedure for Making N-M Diagrams
A general procedure for the intended method for determining the N-M diagram is written.
This
procedure should be common for all different types of stress-strain curves.
1. The minimum strain, εmin and maximum strain, εmax are determined for the specific stress-strain
curve.
2. With the minimum and maximum strains, the position of zero strain, z0 is calculated, as well
as the curvature equations for the three stress cases.
3. For each stress case, z0, κt, and κc are inserted into equations (2.11) - (2.15), calculating N and
M.
4. After determining the equations for the normal force, the central strain, ε0, is isolated for each
stress case. These central strains are inserted into their respective moment equations, calculated
in the earlier step. Now the moment capacity functions for each stress case has been determined.
5. Now, the normal forces, for when the critical cases are switched, are found by inserting equations
(2.18) and (2.19) into the normal force equations, calculated in step 3.
6. Finally, equation (2.20), for the moment capacity of the entire N-M diagram, considering only
positive normal forces, is calculated.
3.4.2
Linear Stress-Strain Curve
The N-M diagram calculations are first performed on the linear stress-strain curve.
Since the stress-strain curve is linear, the minimum and maximum strains are as in equations (3.63)
and (3.64).

is determined to be negative, so the tensile strength, ft, is always a negative value. This report only
concerns positive normal forces in the cross-section, so option 2 will never be applicable. As such,
option 3, given by equation (F.5) is the correct central strain function for case 1.

For normal forces below this interval, the second option will have the strain increase with increasing
normal force. For normal forces above this interval, the first option will have the strain increase with
increasing normal force. The normal force cannot exceed fc ·t as this is the compressive force strength.
Exceeding this would cause crushing of the cross-section. Therefore, option 2, described by equation
(3.93), is the correct central strain function for case 2.
For case 3, the strain is isolated in equation (3.90) and grants two possible solutions expressed by
equations (3.95) and (3.96).

3.4.6
N-M Diagrams
The following section presents the resulting N-M diagrams for the different stress-strain curves, based
on the compressive and tensile strengths. The N-M diagrams will show how the difference in stress-
strain curves will affect the capacities. Along with the N-M diagrams, load points for the two load
scenarios will be shown. These load points take into account the moment contribution from second-
order deflection effects.
Figure 3.16 shows the N-M diagrams for the 3 investigated stress-strain curves, with their correspond-
ing load points, in a situation, where the cross-section is considered uncracked.
(a) Linear
(b) Polynomial
(c) Polynomial (linear tension)
Figure 3.16: N-M diagrams with load points included for Ground floor (blue) and 3rd floor (red).
When looking on Figure 3.16 it is seen that there is a difference in Linear and Polynomial N-M dia-
grams. For the polynomials, the line on the left, indicating tensile capacity, is more curved towards
the transition to compressive capacity, whereas the Linear seems symmetrically linear for each ca-
pacity. Notably, the 3rd floor wall, for all stress-strain curves, is outside the capacity whereas the
ground floor wall lies within the capacity due its larger normal force. When looking at the difference of
polynomial with linear tension versus the purely polynomial no difference can be seen, the difference
is unrecognizable to the naked eye and therefore negligible. From this point forwards the polynomial
with linear tension will be neglected due to its near identicality to the purely polynomial stress-strain
curve and because the corresponding second-order deflections are not determinable.
50
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
==PAGE:55==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
When looking on the difference between the Linear and the Polynomial, Figure 3.17 appears.
Figure 3.17: Comparison of Linear vs Polynomial
On Figure 3.17 it is seen that the peak of graph is slightly skewed to the right for the polynomial. This
means that the linear has a slightly larger moment capacity in the lower normal force range whereas
the polynomial has a larger capacity in the larger forces (near >200 kN/m).
The capacity lines the N-M diagram is made up of, are controlled by material parameters. These
parameters are the compressive and tensile strength. Investigation into the influence of these is done
by varying the strengths. Firstly, on Figure 3.18, the tensile strength has been varied.
(a) Linear
(b) Polynomial
Figure 3.18: N-M diagrams with varying tensile strength. Load points: Ground floor (blue) and 3rd
floor (red).

From Figure 3.18 it is clear to see, for both the linear and the polynomial, that the relation between
increase in tensile strength is linearly proportional with the increase in capacity. When looking at the
loading scenarios it is seen that by increasing the tensile strength to 0.6 MPa the 3rd floor wall is now
within the capacity. The ground floor wall lie well within the capacities, as long as the tensile strength
doesn’t get lower than 0.2 MPa in the Linear model and not below the 0.35 MPa in the Polynomial
model.
When looking at the effect of varying the compressive strength and keeping the tensile strength at
≈0.35 MPa, as expressed by Table 3.6, the results in Figure 3.19 can be seen.

3rd floor (red).
In Figure 3.19a, the increase in compressive capacity seem linear to the increase in compressive
strength.
The same tendency is also seen on Figure 3.19b for the compressive capacity, but an-
other tendency is also seen. The tensile capacity is affected marginally by the compressive strength.
When looking at the effect of compressive strength on the load points it is seen that for the polynomial
stress-strain curve, the maximum moment is slightly lowered with higher compressive strengths. As
such an increase in the compressive stress will lead to an increase in the distance between the moment
capacity and the maximum moments experienced by the cross-section.
As earlier mentioned, in this project, it has been assumed that the tensile strength is one-tenth of the
compressive strength. Keeping this ratio constant while increasing compressive strength will lead to
a simultaneous increase in both strengths. When doing this, Figure 3.20 comes to light.

strength of 6.0 MPa) is needed for the 3rd floor wall to be within the capacity. This highlights the
importance of tensile strength.
Another interesting parameter is the stiffness, governed by the E-modulus. The E-modulus is seen
in equations (3.78) and (3.121) to disappear and have no effect on the N-M diagrams. However, the
stiffness has an effect on the deflection. Increasing deflection will in second-order analysis lead to a
larger induced moment. Figure 3.21 highlights the influence of the E-modulus.

This time, in Figure 3.21 the load point can be seen varying quite significantly based on the elastic
modulus, due to the second-order effects. However, this is only applicable for the ground floor wall
since it needs to have a normal force for the second-order deflections effects to exist. The 3rd floor
wall is therefore seen stationary on the far left outside capacity. Looking at the evolution of the load
point for the ground floor it is seen that it accelerates with a decreasing stiffness and will need an
E-modulus around 500 MPa for the Linear and 600 MPa for the polynomial to stay within the tensile
capacity.
To summarize, it is seen that for the loading scenarios investigated in this project, that the most
influential parameters seems to be tensile strength and E-modulus.
3.5
Cracking of the Cross-section
It may be possible to provide additional capacity to the walls by allowing cracks to form along the
cross-sections. When the cross-section starts cracking, the effective thickness of the wall is reduced.
Reducing the effective cross-section means that there is no cohesion to the cracked section. This means
there is nothing holding the material together at that point, hindering any stresses from being formed.
Figure 3.22 shows this concept.
Figure 3.22: Stresses and strains in cracked cross-section.
This also means that the centre of the effective cross-section is shifted towards the uncracked side
of the wall. However, the normal force applied to the wall will still act at the original centre. This
causes an eccentricity from the effective centre of the cross-section to the acting normal force. This
moment from the eccentric normal force will act against the cracking and will try to close it. Therefore,
an additional moment equal to the moment actions of the eccentric normal force may be applied to
remain at a certain cracking depth.
Naturally, a cross-section that has cracked will have a lower
general stiffness and the critical stresses may be reached faster. Therefore there may be a balance to
be found by letting the cross-section crack, when determining additional capacity.
When the moment, leading to cracking, is positive, the cracks will form on the outside of the wall. The
equations (2.7) and (2.8) determining the internal, and thereby external, normal force and moment
from the stress distribution are then written as in equations (3.141) and (3.142).
N =

In the previous two chapters for it was found that neither the polynomial stress-strain curve with
linear tension nor any ”exponential” stress-strain curve would give proper results for deflection and
max moment. In the case of N-M diagrams, only the polynomial with linear tension would grant a
non-erroneous result. It was also found, however, that the difference between the polynomial with
linear tension, and the purely polynomial stress-strain curve is nigh indistinguishable. As such, no
further research into the cracked case for the polynomial with linear tension and the ”exponential”
curves are shown in the report. N-M diagram capacity calculations may still be read for them in
Appendices [D.2], [D.4], and [D.5].
This means, that for the cracked cross-section, the investigated stress-strain curves are the linear and
the purely 2.-degree polynomial.

3.6
2.-Order Deflections (Cracked)
Allowing the cross-section to crack may grant a higher capacity, but it will also reduce the effective
thickness of the cross-section, which will lead to greater moments and larger 2.-order deflections, which
will further increase the moment imposed on the cross-sections. The thickness of the cross-sections
determine how pliable the walls are to bending, when subjected to external loads. Greater bending will
lead to a greater moment. It is therefore important to know the effective thickness of the cross-section,
when determining the new maximum deflection and moment. The expression for the effective thickness
should optimally be determined as a function of the normal force and moment. Calculating equations
(3.141) and (3.142) (or (3.143) and (3.144) if the moment is negative) and then solving for the central
strain and curvature will grant the necessary information to set up a stress distribution along the
thickness of the wall. If the moment is positive, the point with highest tension is at z = t
2 −teﬀ.
For negative moments, the position is at z = teﬀ−t
2. If the stress function at this position is set
equal to the tensile strength, the effective thickness may be isolated in the equation. This thickness
is now determined as the thickness at which the combination of normal force and moment cause one
side of the cross-section to have reached the tensile strength. If the normal force and moment cause
this thickness to be less than the original thickness of the wall, then this is the effective thickness
after cracking. The moment capacity, M1(N), determined for each stress-strain curve in Chapter 3.4
determines the tensile capacity of the cross-section. Exceeding this will lead to cracking. With this,
equation (3.145) can be set up. This equation describes the general function for the effective thickness
of the cross-section.
teff =

The eccentric normal force caused by cracking gives rise to varying moment actions throughout the
height of the wall. Due to these moments, additional reactions from the supports are formed.
Figure 3.23: Additional reactions caused by cracking of the cross-section.
These reactions will contribute to the moment distribution function along the wall. The additional
term to the moment distribution may be set up in either of two ways. If cutting the wall and looking

3.6.1
General Procedure for Finding Maximum Deflection and Moment
A general procedure for the intended method for determining the maximum 2.-order moment is written.
This procedure should be common for all different types of stress-strain curves.
1. For the given stress-strain curve, calculate equations (3.141) & (3.142) for positive moments and
(3.143) & (3.144) for negative moments to obtain the normal force and moment.
2. Next, the expressions for the central strain and curvature are isolated in the calculated expres-
sions from step 1.
3. The values for central strain and curvature are inserted into the stress-strain function. Then the
z-value is set to the cracked, tensile edge and the stress is set equal to the tensile strength. Then,
isolating for the effective thickness, when assuming cracking, teﬀ,cr, grants the required effective
thickness, where the cross-section reaches the tensile strength for any given normal force and
moment. Equation (3.145) can now be set up for both positive and negative moments. Since the
cross-section is symmetrical around its centreline, even when cracked, the result for the effective
thickness will be the same, regardless of the moment being positive or negative.
4. The expression for the effective thickness is inserted into the curvature function determined in
step 2. Then the unknown moment, M, is replaced with the moment distribution along the
height of the wall, given by equation (3.148).
5. Then the curvature function is integrated as in equation (2.1). The boundary conditions in
equation (3.16) are now used to obtain the expressions for the two integration constants and the
additional reaction, from cracking, at the top of the wall.
6. If possible, the maximum 2.-order moment for the cracked cross-section, is now determined by
using the same procedure as steps 5 - 8 for the uncracked situation in Chapter 3.3.1.

3.6.4
Application of Calculations
When calculating second-order deflections and moments for the cracked cross-section, using a linear
stress-strain curve, it is found, that the calculations cannot be performed on the 3rd floor wall. The
wall is constantly in a state of instability. As such, the results for the linear stress-strain curve is only
shown for the ground floor.
The load point, for the ground floor, in Figure 3.16a is shown to be under the capacity which means
no cracking should form. This leads to the reaction from cracking should be zero. Due to the use
of numerical sum-approximation of the integrals, some deviation may occur, which leads to an offset
from the zero deflection of the top of the wall. This deviation can be adjusted for, by inserting a
corrective proper cracking reaction. Table 3.12 compares the result of the second-order analysis for
an unadjusted and an adjusted cracking reaction for a cross-section with allowed cracking

3.7
Cross-Sectional Capacity Analysis (Cracked)
The relation between moment and curvature leads to a useful observation. If the curvature at a cross-
section can be increased and still remain within the capacity of the cross-section, so can the moment.
The maximum curvature achievable, without the cross-section failing, is when the compressive stresses
at one end reach the compressive strength and at the same time the stresses in the other end reach the
tensile strength. Translated into strains, this means that the strains go from the minimum allowable
in one end, to the maximum allowable in the other end.
When the normal force is lowered, the
central strain is also lowered, which means that the strains at the compressive side no longer reaches
the maximum capacity.
To compensate for this, the curvature must be further increased.
This
however will also increase the tension at the other end, pushing it beyond its capacity. The part of
the cross-section, where the tensile capacity is exceeded will now crack, reducing the effective size
of the cross-section. Now the effective cross-section will, however fulfil the condition of minimum to
maximum stress. This will be described as stress case 4. Figure 3.29 shows a visual representation of
this stress-case.
Figure 3.29: Fourth critical stress case for cross-section capacity.
The position of the crack tip, zmin, for any given combination of central strain and curvature, is found
by setting the linear strain function, from equation (2.9), equal to the minimum allowable strain and
then isolating the z-value. This grants equation (3.182)
zmin = εmin −ε0
κ
(3.182)
For case 4 to be fulfilled, the curvature must give rise to the stresses reaching the compressive stress
at the opposite end of the cross-section. This compression is reached by using equation (2.17) for the
curvature. Inserting equation (2.17) into equation (3.182), the cracking tip position is determined for
stress case 4, as a function of the central strain. This function is shown in equation (3.183).
zmin = t

3.7.1
General Procedure for Making N-M Diagrams
A general procedure for the intended method for determining the N-M diagram is written.
This
procedure should be common for all different types of stress-strain curves.
1. The minimum strain, εmin and maximum strain, εmax are determined for the specific stress-strain
curve.
2. With the minimum and maximum strains, the position of zero strain, z0 and of the crack tip
zmin is calculated, as well as the curvature equation for the stress cases 2, 3, and 4.
3. For stress cases 2 and 3, z0 and κc are inserted into equations (2.11) - (2.15)
4. For stress case 4, zmin, z0, and κc are inserted into equations (3.184) - (3.185) or (3.186) - (3.187).
5. After determining the equations for the normal force, the central strain, ε0, is isolated for each
stress case. These central strains are inserted into their respective moment equations, calculated
in steps 3 and 4. Now, the moment capacity functions for each stress case have been determined.
6. Now, the normal forces, for when the critical cases are switched, are found by inserting equations
(3.188) and (2.19) into the normal force equations, calculated in steps 3 and 4.
7. Finally, equation (3.189), for the moment capacity of the entire N-M diagram, considering only
positive normal forces, is calculated.

3.7.4
N-M Diagrams
When taking into account that the cross-section is allowed to crack, the N-M diagram is no longer
limited by the tensile capacity alone. Instead, it reflects a new stress case where both tensile and
compressive strain limits may be active, as shown for Case 4 in Figure 3.29.
This allows for an
increase in capacity compared to the uncracked case. Going beyond the top line of the uncracked
diagram leads to progressive cracking in tension and eventually crushing in compression. Figure 3.30,
compares the uncracked and cracked N-M diagrams for the linear and polynomial stress-strain curves.
In both cases, the cracked (blue) curves clearly show increased capacity relative to the uncracked
(red) ones. This demonstrates that taking into account the ability of the material to yield and crack
provides a clear structural capacity benefit, especially under combined axial load and bending.
(a) Linear
(b) Polynomial
Figure 3.30: Cracked and uncracked N-M diagrams including the load points for Ground floor (blue)
and 3rd floor (red).
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
73
==PAGE:78==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
Another key aspect is how the state of cracking evolves along the cracked curve. At the lower end
of normal force (left side of the curve), the wall is dominated by bending, which results in significant
tension on one side of the section. This is where the cross-section is most severely cracked. As the
normal force increases, more of the section enters compression, reducing the level of cracking. Along
the curved blue lines of the diagrams, both tensile and compressive stress limits are active. Moving
beyond the peak on the N-axis, the section is increasingly governed by crushing in compression rather
than tension.
This behaviour shows that the cracked N-M curve represents not only the improved capacity, but
also the evolution from tension-dominated cracking to compression-dominated crushing. The position
along the cracked curve thus indicates how damaged the material is, with the far left being fully
cracked, and the right side being no cracking only crushing.
When overlapping the linear and the polynomial, it is seen in Figure 3.31 that the polynomial covers
the linear. This does not directly mean that Linear is more critical since Polynomial has a bigger
effect on the placement of the load point for the ground floor, since the larger deflections result in
larger moments.
Figure 3.31: Comparison of Linear vs Polynomial - Cracked cross-section.
When varying the tensile strength to see the effect on the capacity, it can be seen from Figure 3.32,
that there is nearly no extra contribution to the capacity if the section is cracked. There is a little
capacity to gain when at the switch point between stress case 4 and 2. There is virtually no capacity
increase in the region of normal forces where the load-points are placed. This makes it so the 3rd floor
wall won’t be able to get within the capacity.

3.8
Thicker Wall
In the previous sections, some problems of the applied methods were identified, particularly in relation
to cracking. One possible idea to why this might be happening is instability due to the slenderness of
the wall. To test this, an increase the thickness of the wall will be investigated. A thicker wall can
provide better resistance to deflections, which could reduce the risk of instability failure by keeping
the wall within a more stable deformation range. All theory remains the same; the only difference is
the new thickness and the adjusted loads.
An investigation is therefore done to check the influence of the wall thickness by doubling the wall’s
thickness to 216 mm in total. In this section, only selected results are presented to highlight the effect
of increased wall thickness while avoiding repetitive data.
3.8.1
New Loads
The first things to be determined are the new loads, since a thicker wall leads to an increase in
deadload. The loading scenarios are the same just with added deadload for the ground floor wall. The
load scenario for the 3rd floor wall remains unchanged. The resulting forces for the load scenarios can
be found in Table 3.15 and Table 3.16. Detailed calculations are found in Appendix [A.1].
Table 3.15: Design values for 216 mm wall (Ground Floor).

The extra width adds to the overall stiffness of the wall, aiding to resist bending, leading to lower
deflections. The absolute maximum deflections can be seen illustrated in Figure 3.36 and the numerical
values are set up in Table 3.17.

When looking at the effect of thickness on the capacities of the N-M diagrams, on Figure 3.37, a
significant increase in moment capacity is seen, compared to the 108 mm wall which peaked at around
a moment of 4 kN for the Linear instance and 4.5 kN for the Polynomial as seen on Figure 3.30. By
doubling the thickness of the wall the capacity has been quadrupled. Both investigated load scenarios
are now well within the uncracked region and are positioned well below the tensile strength limit of
the material. It is also seen varying the E-moduli has a very little effect. The load points are barely
moving, since the deflections have become significantly smaller than for the 108 mm wall.

3.9
Finite Element Model
A finite element model has been investigated. The use of this model is to try and verify the theoretical
model. The walls have been modelled and subjected to loads to analyse its structural behaviour under
the different loading scenarios. As mentioned, it has been assumed that even though the experimental
data comes from masonry, the wall behaves as a homogeneous material. This assumption aids in
streamlining the finite element analysis with the theoretical model while still providing reasonable
approximations for the structural behaviour of the walls.
3.9.1
General Setup of an ABAQUS FEM Model
To validate the theoretical structural behaviour of the walls, a numerical model was established using
the ABAQUS software in Standard mode, which is primarily used for static investigations.
This
chapter outlines the essential steps and reasoning behind the configuration of the model, including
part definition, material modelling, steps, load application, boundary conditions, and meshing strategy.
Units: Prior to all setup, complementary units had to be chosen. SI-units have been chosen, which
means: Dimensions in metres, loads in N or Pa and stresses in Pa. This leads to results from deflections
in metres and stresses in pascal.
Part Definition and Geometry: The structures under investigation are four instances of load-
bearing walls with dimensions 3 m x 6 m x (108 mm or 216 mm), as described in the previous sections
of the report. In ABAQUS, the wall is modelled as a three-dimensional deformable solid extrusion part.
This representation captures the out-of-plane effects and stress distributions, which are essential for
investigating phenomena such as second-order deflections and failures. A homogeneous solid ”Section”
is assigned to the part, meaning that the entire geometry is assumed to consist of a single, continuous
material with uniform properties.
This simplification is acceptable when no composite or layered
behaviour is intended to be modelled.
Material Definition: The material behaviour was assigned to the part depending on whether a
linear, non-linear or damaged non-linear simulation was intended. The material model governs how
the wall responds to stresses and strains throughout the analysis. Material models used in this project
will be explained later in this chapter
Step and Load Application: To allow boundary conditions to take effect prior to loading, a separate
”Static General Step” was created, following the ”Initial step”, and called the loading step. The initial
step is reserved for defining constraints such as boundary conditions that remain constant throughout
the simulation. In the loading step, loading was applied according to the calculated design loads, with
consideration of eccentricity for the normal force and wind pressure where relevant. Applying loads in
a later step ensures proper load propagation, since the boundary conditions have had a step to settle.
Loads was applied as ”pressure loads” with precautions by using ”Total load” which means the total
force is applied as newton and then equally distributed.
In ABAQUS, time increment control is also controlled in the ”Step” module. This allows for controlling
the total time and either ”fixed” or ”automatic” increments. ”Time” can be used as a tool to advance
towards convergence of the model. Automatic increments adapt based on convergence behaviour to
ensure stable, eﬀicient simulation. Another thing that is added in the step module is ”NLGEOM”
which is Non-linear Geometry this is to account for large deflection, by taking into account second-
order deflection effects.
80
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
==PAGE:85==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
Boundary Conditions: Boundary conditions were imposed to replicate the support conditions
described in the theoretical model.
For example, the base of the wall was fully fixed to simu-
late a fixed support with no rotations (UR1=UR2=UR3=0) and no displacements in any direction
(U1=U2=U3=0). The top was simply supported with permission for translation in the vertical di-
rection along with rotation around the y-axis (UR1=UR3=U2=U3=0) to mimic realistic structural
behaviour. These constraints are essential to correctly capture stress and deformation patterns in the
wall.
Meshing Strategy: The part was meshed using custom meshing techniques.
A finer mesh was
employed through the thickness of the wall to ensure accuracy in stress, strain, and deflection results.
This approach aid in reducing errors due to large changes in stresses and strain across elements. This
also help capture the gradient of stress, strain and deflection across the section more precise. The
chosen element type, from the ABAQUS library, was the C3D8R. This was chosen due to it not
requiring much computational power while still leading to suﬀiciently accurate results. The C3D8R is
an 8-node linear brick element with reduced integration and hourglass control. The cubic shape fits
perfectly to the simple cubic wall. The reduced integration means that the 8 nodes integration points
has been reduced to one integration point in the middle of the element. This integration point has 3
translational DOFs meaning it only account for movement of the point not rotations. Rotation will
appear when points move in relation to each other. This element type is suitable for modelling solids
undergoing deformations and is commonly used in concrete and masonry simulations [10].
After meshing, a mesh verification process in ABAQUS was conducted to identify any inconsistencies
or errors in element definition.
No issues were detected, indicating that the mesh was correctly
generated.
3.9.2
Material Model Types Implemented
In this small section detailed descriptions of the material model setups are entailed.
Linear-Elastic Model: For baseline comparison, a linear-elastic model was implemented.
This
model required input of the E-modulus and Poisson’s ratio. In accordance with the theoretical model
not taking lateral strains into account, Poisson’s ratio was set to zero, simplifying the behaviour. As
such, only the elastic modulus was necessary for defining the material.
To investigate the influence of stiffness on the 2.-order deflection, multiple elastic moduli were tested
in the range: 300-1200 MPa. These values cover a realistic range around the experimental estimate
(590.6 MPa) for the tested wall material.
Table 3.18: Linear-Elastic Model Parameters and Material Properties.
Parameter
Symbol
Value
Note
Elastic modulus

300-1200 MPa

Poisson’s ratio
