Consistent with project model
Concrete Damage Plasticity: To simulate material behaviour beyond the elastic limit, the Concrete
Damaged Plasticity (CDP) model was chosen. This model allows representation for both compressive
plasticity and tensile damage in concrete-like materials. CDP includes several constant parameters,
as seen in Table 3.19, which influence the plastic flow and damage evolution. The model is combined
with the elastic model to explain the initial elastic zone prior to plasticity. The parameters were
selected based on a literature study.
The study found, performed testing and validation of finite
element modelling on rammed earth construction [11]. An assumption here is then that unfired clay
and rammed earth match in expected material behaviour.

For improved convergence
Normally the viscosity parameter is set to 0 but to ensure numerical stability and enhance conver-
gence, a small viscosity parameter of 0.0001 was introduced. This introduces a slight rate dependency
(loading ramp speed), but improves robustness in non-linear simulations [12]. Another thing that was
added in the non-linear model to improve convergence was the step time. The step time was increased
from 1 (Default) to 8 to allow the wall enough time to find equilibrium. The minimum step size was
reduced to 10-7 from 10-5 (Default) to allow the model to take smaller steps to ensure accuracy and
convergence.
Preparation of input (Undamaged): The tabulated input data can be found in Appendix [E.1]
for different E-moduli (300, 590.6 and 900).
To define the CDP model in ABAQUS, the material behaviour was described using true stress-strain
relationships for both compression and tension, according to the stress strain curve for the respective
E-modulus. The following steps were taken to prepare the material input:
First, the experimental stress-strain data were converted from engineering values to true stress and
true strain, following equations (2.21) and (2.22).
Once true values had been obtained, the tabulated inelastic strains were determined for the compres-
sive post-yield. This was done by subtracting the elastic strain from the total strain, following the
procedure described equation (2.23). The resulting tabulated inelastic strain values were input along
with the corresponding true stress.
For tension, a linear-elastic response was assumed up to a peak tensile strength of approximately
0.35 MPa. Beyond this point, a linear softening slope was used to describe the amount of plastic
deformation. The cracking strain values were determined using the same approach as for compression,
equation (2.23), and the results were input with the corresponding tensile stress.
After these inputs have been defined, ABAQUS automatically evaluates the results, when running a
”Job”.
Preparation of input (Damaged): Same preparation as for the undamaged instance was done until
the point of defining tension behaviour. The theory used in the undamaged no longer applies since
it revolves around having reinforcement. The fracture energy criterion was therefore used instead.
For tension a fracture energy of 10 N/m was assumed which is one fourth of a typical concrete with
82

Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
strength of 20 MPa [2]. This estimate had to be made due to lack of test determining fracture energy
on unfired clay-like materials. With the known tensile strength of 0.3478 MPa the maximum cracking
deflection, ucr from equation (2.27).
ucr =

The data was then tabulated as seen in Sheet ”Fracture criterion (590.MPa)” in Appendix [E.1]. Af-
ter countless trials and errors of varying fracture energy, viscosity parameter along with other fixes
from the internet nothing seamed to help this model converge. The model file can be found in Ap-
pendix [E.2]. The model is only here to validate the theoretical model and it was therefore deemed
that another method had to be tried.
Considering the undamaged CDP model converged without substantial diﬀiculties, the reinforced
model method was chosen again. This is not completely viable when looking at cracking since re-
inforcement would prevent further crack propagation and might lead to extreme localisations (high
local stress concentrations). But in the effort to try and get some results for validation it was tried.
The exact tabulated values from the undamaged was then used along with determined damage pa-
rameters. The damage evolution was enabled and described using the damage parameters dc and dt,
corresponding to the damage in compression and tension, respectively. These values were determined
with the approximation in equation (2.26), and were input alongside the respectively inelastic strain
and cracking strain.

3.9.4
CDP - Undamaged
Before the data for the CDP model can be set up, yield criteria has to be determined. For tension,
this was simply the tensile strength of ≈0.35 MPa. For compression, yield was defined as the point
where the stress-strain response for the polynomial deviates 5% from the linear assumption. This
corresponds to:
σ = 80
441 · σmax ≈0.631 MPa
for σmax = 3.4779MPa
Following the small preparation of input from this chapter the following things were done. The poly-
nomial stress-strain curve was used to generate data for the post-yield behaviour in compression and
converted to true stresses and strains. The inelastic curve was then shifted such that the zero inelastic
strain corresponds to the point of yield. Cracking strain for the linear post-peak tensile region was
determined. All values were input into the respective compressive and tensile behaviour tabs of the
CDP model. The final values input can be found in Appendix [E.1]. While a full range of elastic
moduli from 300 to 1200 MPa has been analysed in the Linear-elastic model, a selection of three
values for comparison has been selected (300, 590.6, and 900 MPa).

The results for the plastic strain confirm that permanent deformations have happened either by yield-

hand, permanent deformations are only found in the bottom near support where tensile capacity have

4
Discussion
4.1
Theoretical modelling:
Homogeneous Assumption:
When examining the shape of the stress–strain curves, the system under consideration consists of a
composite material made from unfired clay bricks and a thin mortar layer (3 mm). For the purposes
of calculation, a simplification has been applied by assuming the wall is homogeneous. This assump-
tion is considered reasonable due to the minimal thickness of the mortar joints. In compression, this
is likely a valid approximation, as the response will primarily reflect the behaviour of the weakest
component in the composite. However, in tension, additional effects such as the bonding strength
between the unfired clay bricks and the mortar may become significant, and these are neglected in
the simplification. Upon the initiation of cracking, particularly due to bending, cracks are expected
to form preferentially at the joints, which is likely to occur in practice. Nevertheless, the analysis
proceeds under the assumption that the material behaves as a unified whole and that the bond is
suﬀiciently strong to justify this approach.
Applicability of Stress-Strain Curve Models:
Despite its versatility to behave like the experimentally found stress-strain curve, the ”exponential”
stress-strain curve shows very little versatility, when actually used in calculations. Immediately, when
trying to employ equations (2.7) and (2.8) with this stress-strain curve, the result becomes non-
analytical. Furthermore it is seemingly impossible to isolate an expression for the curvature based on
applied normal forces and moments. This means a deflection function, and subsequently any second-
order calculations seem unobtainable. It also appears that when determining a capacity function for
the moment based on the normal force, the function becomes non-analytical and has to be solved
numerically. The conditions at which the stress cases switch to the next have, however, been calcu-
lated analytically, so there is only a problem with determining a relation between the moment and
the normal force.
The ”exponential” stress-strain curve has been calculated both with a linear tensile section and by
continuing the ”exponential” curve into the tensile region. The problems encountered are the exact
same. As such the ”exponential” stress-strain curve appears to not be worth pursuing much further,
if the goal is to determine fully analytical expressions with clear relations between parameters.
The second most complex stress-strain curve is the second-degree polynomial one, where the tensile
region is set as linear. With this, the capacity functions in the N-M diagrams, both uncracked and
cracked can be determined fully analytically. However, the simplicity of calculation only occur in the
critical cases, there the strengths of the material is fully utilised. For the deflection calculations, a
prerequisite is to be able to calculate actions in general cases, where the resulting strains and curva-
tures are not at their max. Here it becomes impossible to obtain fully analytical expressions. The
function for the curvature must be acquired numerically, which means all second-order results will
need numerical approximation.
When going with stress-strain curve that is fully second-degree polynomial, It is possible to advance
further with the calculations of the second-order deflections and moment. The blockade to progress
towards analytical solutions, here appears to be the integration from curvature to deflection along the
wall. As it may be mathematically impossible to analytically convert the curvature into deflection, the
only way to obtain results is through numerical summation. It appears to be possible to also make an
expression for the effective thickness of a cracked cross-section. The boundaries for this function has
not yet been solved analytically, though. As such it is possible to make curvature functions for both
98
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
==PAGE:103==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
cracked and uncracked cross-sections without relying on numerical methods. Doing all N-M diagram
calculations analytically is also entirely feasible.
The seemingly simplest stress-strain curve possible, the linear, is the one that can provide the most
analytical results. All N-M diagram calculations are simple and analytical and it is possible to ob-
tain a deflection function entirely analytically. It is almost fully possible to obtain the second-order
maximum deflection, but the final step requires solving a fifth degree polynomial, which requires
non-analytical tools. Furthermore, integrating the curvature equation for the cracked cross-section is
seemingly not mathematically possible, but needs further investigation.
It appears that in general, when the stress-strain curve consists of two different functions, the com-
plexity will increase rapidly, when attempting to perform deflection analyses. It is thus suggested that
if the results do not vary to any noticeable degree, it is better to calculate stress-strain curves, where
the compressive and tensile parts are explained by the same function. Analytical calculations should
only become more feasible this way.
Computational Complexity of Second-Order Effects and Capacity Calculations:
It has been found that performing calculations for N-M diagrams for the stress in the cross-section of
an element is generally results in analytical expressions. Curiously, these calculations become simpler
when allowing the cross-section to crack.
It is generally more diﬀicult to obtain equations for the deflection along the height of the wall; cur-
rently the only analytical result being from the linear stress-strain curve. Determining the second-order
deflections, however, proves more diﬀicult even, when assuming that the moment from deflection of
the centreline is constant throughout the wall. It has been found that at the current simplifications,
second-order calculations, will indisputably require numerical calculations. In the case of deflection
calculations, allowing cracking will only lead to more complexity and unknowns to be solved. Due
to the varying cracking along the height of the wall, the additional reactions from the supports are
unable to be determined analytically. Even if the effective thickness is set as constant throughout
the height of the wall, it will still lead to requiring a numerical solution to determine the effective
thickness, similar to when determining the maximum deflection.
As such to obtain capacity results based purely on the theory used in this report, numerical solutions
cannot be avoided.
Comparison of Stress-Strain Curve Model Results:
The difference between the two polynomial stress-strain curves appear to be miniscule. Figure 3.16
shows the uncracked capacity diagrams side by side. Here there is no visible difference between them.
In Appendix [D.2] the diagrams for varying the different parameters as in the report are shown for the
polynomial stress-strain curve with linear tension. Here, too, do the diagrams appear the same with
no discernible difference. Although there is no results for the 2.-order calculations, they are assumed
to behave similarly. Thus the purely polynomial stress-strain curve is assumed to be a good estimate
for the polynomial stress-strain curve with linear tension.
Throughout the results a general pattern emerges. The linear stress-strain curve tends to show less
critical results than the polynomial stress-strain curves. Figure 3.17 shows that, at larger normal
forces, the linear stress-strain curve is on the safe side, however at medium to low normal forces,
which is where the load points are situated, the capacity for the polynomial stress-strain curve is the
lowest. This is the same trend for all uncracked capacity diagrams, regardless of the parameter being
varied. In regards to deflection, the linear stress-strain curve requires more energy to deflect, so the
deflections are all less than for the polynomial stress-strain curve. In Table 3.10 it is also shown, that
instability occurs much later than for polynomial stress-strain curves. As such, when the cross-section
is uncracked, the results from the linear stress-strain curve is on the unsafe side and overestimates
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
99
==PAGE:104==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
the capacity. Curiously, when the cross-section is allowed to crack the capacity shown by the N-M
diagrams is always larger for the polynomial stress strain curve. This may be caused by the flattening
of the stress-strain curve requiring larger strains to reach the compressive capacity. Since the energy
required to deflect the wall is still inherently lower for polynomial stress-strain curves, it is assumed
that 2.-order deflections and moments will be larger for polynomial stress-strain curves than linear.
As such, the instability and load points may be more critically placed in reality than what is given by
Figure 3.35.
Comparison of Cracked and Uncracked Cross-Sections:
It has also been shown that there is capacity to be gained, when allowing the cross-section to crack.
At lower values, though, the cracked capacity is lower than the uncracked capacity, as shown in Figure
3.30. This is easily explained by the thickness of the effective cross-section being too small to make
up for the increase in curvature.
When the cracked moment capacity is greater than the uncracked, the region above the uncracked
capacity line is where the cross-section cracks. The greater the moment is, the longer the crack be-
comes, until the moment reaches the cracked capacity line. This is the smallest cross-section thickness
allowable for the given normal force, before the stresses cannot remain in equilibrium. Surpassing
the cracked capacity, will cause the crack to go through the entire cross-section immediately. On the
other hand, when the cracked capacity is lower than the uncracked capacity, the cross-section should
not be allowed to crack. Exceeding this line means that both capacities are exceeded at once and the
crack will immediately go through the whole cross-section, splitting the wall in two separate pieces.
As such extra attention should be placed on not allowing the uncracked capacity to be exceeded at
low normal forces, while high normal forces some leeway.
Accuracy of Numerical Summation Method:
In Appendix [C.13], the numerical calculations for second-order deflection and moment are carried out
for the expressions where the curvature functions can not be integrated. As an approximation to the
integration, summation, that partitions the height of the wall into steps, is used. The more partitions
that are made, the more precise the approximation becomes. Each partition is 1 mm long, so the
summation along the entire 3 m tall wall is performed in 3000 steps. This may result in minor errors
or deviations from the full integration. An example is the precision of the maximum deflection. It is
unlikely, that the position along the height of the wall for the maximum deflection falls exactly on the
millimetre. It may be that the actual maximum deflection falls between two steps. Here the highest
deflection value of the two steps is considered the maximum deflection, despite the true maximum
deflection being slightly larger. The same goes when determining the moment. As such, the presented
max deflections will be slightly lower than the actual max values. However, since the steps are so
small, the difference is assumed to be insignificant.
Additional Reaction Force from Cracking Dilemma:
Another problem that arises from the summation approximation is the error from zero deflection at
the top support. Table 3.12 presents this problem well. The load point investigated is at the ground
floor wall and the moment does not give rise to cracking. As there is no cracking, there will be no
shift in the effective centre of the cross-section, leading to no additional moment. This means that
there should not be any extra reaction from the supports. However, due to the inaccuracy of the
summation alternative to integration, the deflection at the top of the wall becomes non-zero. The
boundary conditions state, that the deflection at the top must be zero and the additional reaction from
cracking, RL,cr is supposed to ensure this. The dilemma becomes that the reaction should be set, so
that the boundary conditions are kept, however, the cross-section is cracked, so it should also remain
100
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
==PAGE:105==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
at zero. The values of deflection and moment can be compared to those given in Table 3.7. For the
linear stress-strain curve at ground floor, these calculations are carried out in Appendix [C.13], where
the analytical integration has been successfully performed. This means, that the values, for the linear
stress-strain curve, are the correct values based on the theory. All the values here suit the results
where the reaction has been adjusted for the boundary conditions much better, than if it remained at
zero. In truth, the difference between adjusting for boundary conditions and not doing so is negligible.
Using either will not lead to any perceivable difference nor change in capacity control. As such it is
for consistency’s sake that all calculations of the additional reaction, are made with adjustment in
accordance to the boundary conditions.
Important Capacity Parameters:
It becomes apparent from Figure 3.16, that the load points all reside near the tensile capacity region.
There is a lot of space to apply more compression, without risking any crushing failures. As such it is
clear, that when trying to optimise the material or the design of walls made of unfired clay, the tensile
capacity must be the main focus. Performing changes to the material that reduces the compressive
capacity to gain more tensile capacity should not be a worry.
Compressive Strengths Influence on Capacity:
However, even if the compressive ’capacity’ can be adjusted freely without worry, the same does not
go for the compressive ’strength’ of the material. Figures 3.19 and 3.34 show that the compressive
strength of the material has some influence over the tensile capacity of the cross-section. This effect
is most pronounced at larger normal forces and gets more muted near the lower end, where the load
points, for the critical load cases on the building, are located. At lower compressive strengths, this
effect also seem to be amplified. In Figure 3.19b it is seen, that for the ground floor normal force,
the contribution from increasing the strength from 3.48 MPa to 6.0 MPa is barely noticeable, while
the change from 3.478 MPa to 2.0 MPa has a greater impact on the tensile capacity curve. It is also
found that for the second-degree polynomial stress-strain curve, which is the one most similar to the
experimental stress-strain curve, the compressive strength has an impact on the second-order moment.
The higher the strength is, the more the moment is reduced. This effect, though small, is very relevant
for the capacity for the cross-section. When lowering the compressive strength the moment capacity
is decreased, while the moment itself is increased. This means, that already at a compressive strength
down at 2.0 MPa, the tensile capacity has been exceeded, without even doing anything to the tensile
strength. Furthermore, this is for a wall at the bottom of a 4 storey building, so lower buildings will
be even more affected by low strengths. As such. If one wishes to make changes to the material such
that the strength changes, lowering the compressive stress significantly should be avoided, unless the
tensile strength is guaranteed to increase accordingly.
Capacity Interaction between Compressive and Tensile Strength:
Figures 3.20b and 3.34b can be used to check if there is some kind of interaction between the compres-
sive and tensile strengths when they are both changed. If there is no special interaction, the change
in tensile and compressive capacity can be determined by first looking at Figures 3.19b and 3.33b
while changing the compressive strength, then looking at Figures 3.18b and 3.32b, while changing
the tensile strength. With no interaction, the total change in capacity will just be the sum of each
change from each strength. Figures 3.19b and 3.33b make it clear that there is some interaction. To
get a grasp of this interaction, Figures 3.18b and 3.19b can be directly compared to Figure 3.20b,
while Figures 3.32b and 3.33b can be directly compared to Figure 3.34b. It is found, that the differ-
ence between the sum of the individual changes and changing the strengths together is insignificant
at lower normal forces. and for greater strengths, this zone of insignificant difference is increased.
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
101
==PAGE:106==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
This means, that for the normal force at the ground floor wall, if the compressive strength is reduced
from 3.478 MPa to 2.0 MPa, Figure 3.18b shows that the tensile strength should be increased from
-0.348 MPa to -0.5 MPa in order to accordingly compensate for the reduction in tensile capacity. Nat-
urally, if the aim is to reduce the risk of cracking, the tensile strength should be increased even further.
The Effect of E-modulus:
Equations 3.78, 3.101, and 3.121 all show, that the elastic modulus has no effect on the cross-sectional
capacity of the cross-section. However, it has been found, that the E-modulus has a great impact
on the 2.-order deflection and moment. Lowering the E-modulus will make both the deflections and
moments rise at an increased rate, while increasing it lowers them at a reducing rate. Figure 3.21
shows the volatility of the effect, low E-moduli have. Already at an E-modulus of 500 MPa, the tensile
crack-free capacity is exceeded. Meanwhile, the change from 900 MPa to 1200 MPa is miniscule. As
such, though the potential decrease in the maximum moment is notable when going from low to high
E-moduli, it is a more crucial concern, that the E-modulus is not lowered. With the current wall
thickness and material strengths, if one would attempt to optimise the material, measures should be
taken, that the E-modulus should only be increased. Increasing it above 900 MPa will be redundant,
as the gain in safety is insignificant after this point.
Curiously, the load point, at the 3rd floor wall, where the normal force is zero, does not appear to vary,
based on the E-modulus or compressive strength. This may simply be explained by the lack of 2.-order
effects. The 2.-order effects come to be, through the additional moments, caused by the combination
of deflection and its perpendicular normal force. If there is no normal force, there is no second-order
moment effect. Figures 3.11b and 3.12b shows, that even if the maximum moment remains constant,
the deflections do not. This sparks an interesting observation. Equation 3.164 shows that, when the
normal force is zero, the effective thickness only relies on the moment and tensile strength. This
means that while the deflection may approach an infinite size, due to low E-modulus or compressive
strength, there may not be any cracks forming, since the moment from transverse loads can be lower
than the cracking capacity. Naturally it is expected that at high deflection, some type of failure will
occur. Lower E-moduli means that the stress build-up in the cross-section requires larger stresses, so
it is expected that the wall may deflect to some degree without cracking.
Instability of Load Points in Low Normal Forces:
It was also found that the deflection calculations were not possible to be performed on the cracked
cross-section for the 3rd floor wall. The reason for this, is found in Figure 3.30. At no normal force, the
uncracked capacity is higher than the critical cracked capacity. This means that if the cross-section
cracks due to exceeding the uncracked capacity, the moment must now be lowered in order to reach a
statical equilibrium. The moment, however is not dependent on the effective thickness at zero normal
force. Furthermore, when a cross-section cracks, and the normal force is not zero, 2.-order effects will
only lead to an increase in the maximum moment, not a decrease. The only way for the cross-section
to reach statical equilibrium, is if the cross-section is cracked before reaching the moment capacity
for an uncracked cross-section. This scenario is, however, not covered in this report, as it relates to
either already damaged elements at production or elements damaged from dynamic loading. As such
it is not possible to express a 2.-order deflection or moment for normal forces, where the uncracked
capacity is larger than the cracked capacity.
102
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
==PAGE:107==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
Load Points Accuracy Outside Capacities:
The deflections and moments shown in the figures and tables of Chapter 3.3.6 all assume uncracked
cross-sections. This poses no problem until the maximum moments exceed the moment capacity for
uncracked cross-sections. This means that the inaccuracy of the models are fast increasing, the larger
the crack propagation becomes. Changes to the design of the wall and strengths of the material may
push the cross-section to become uncracked, but would also change the results of the figures. As such,
the results from Chapter 3.3.6 should only be trusted as long as the cross-section remains uncracked.
When the uncracked capacity is exceeded, they should only be considered with high scepticism.
Limits to Lowering the Cross-Sectional Moment:
When the figures in Chapters 3.3.6 and 3.6.4 show that increasing the E-modulus or compressive
strength lowers the maximum deflection and moment, it should be noted that the moment is not
reduced indefinitely. The E-modulus determines the initial slope of the stress-strain curve, while,
for the polynomial stress-strain curve, the compressive strength determines how quickly the curve
flattens out. Together, they describe the energy required to increase the strain in the cross-section a
certain amount, thus deflecting the wall. When the compressive strength nears infinity, the energy
will become the same for the polynomial stress-strain curve, as for the linear. The E-modulus is most
crucial, as, when it nears infinity, it will require an infinitely large energy to make the wall deflect
at all. This means the maximum deflection will be at zero. This has the same effect on the 2.-order
moment as setting the normal force to zero, namely there will be no second-order effect. Thus, in
the tables and figures in Chapters 3.3.6 and 3.6.4, increasing the E-modulus will eventually lead the
deflections to zero and the moments to those which are caused by the transverse loading.
Instability Reached Before Critical Moment Capacity:
Other effects happen, when the E-modulus is brought as low as possible. As is shown in Tables 3.10
and 3.14, there will be a point, where the E-modulus becomes so low, that there is no resistance
against deflecting infinitely. When the cross-section is assumed to never crack, the deflections and
moments at which this occurs are very large and far beyond the capacities set by the N-M diagrams.
This means that at least for the given two load points, the stability of the wall is never a concern of
greater import than the stress capacities. However the E-modulus in Table 3.14, where the cracking of
the cross-section is considered, results in a much lower maximum moment. In Figure 3.35 it is shown
that this moment is below the cracked cross-sectional moment capacity. This means that despite the
capacity diagram showing the moment can be larger, the instability at an elastic modulus of 423 MPa
limits the actual moment capacity. This is merely based on the linear stress-strain curve. The poly-
nomial, which is closer to the actual stress-strain curve, requires less energy to deflect and as such the
moment of instability may be even further reduced.
This is a clear indication that the N-M diagrams calculated based on the stresses in the cross-section
do not fully indicate the true capacities and should take instability into account.
Unless a third
axis is included in the capacity diagram to account for E-moduli, it will become necessary to make
new N-M diagrams, where instability is also accounted for, for each relevant E-modulus. Naturally,
the E-modulus is only one facet of what could instigate instability. Many other factors such as how
the external loads act on the wall, thickness of the cross-section, height of the wall, etc., also play a
role. If a truly general capacity diagram should be made, the N-M diagram will likely not be suﬀicient.
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
103
==PAGE:108==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
Increased Wall Thickness:
The results clearly demonstrate that increasing the wall thickness has a substantial impact on both
the structural response and the numerical robustness of the model. By doubling the thickness from
108 mm to 216 mm, the overall stiffness increases significantly, thereby reducing deflections under
load. This directly improves stability and aids in keeping the wall within the undamaged range, which
explains why the deflection curves for the linear and polynomial material models are almost identical
on the 3rd floor wall.
On the ground floor, where second-order effects are present, the thicker wall still shows a smaller
increase of 10% in deflection, when comparing the linear and polynomial stress-strain curves. However,
this is a smaller relative difference than seen for the thinner wall, suggesting that the increased stiffness
reduces non-linearity effects, due to reducing the second-order effects.
The N-M diagrams further support this interpretation. The moment capacity, at peak, has increased
by nearly a factor of four. Additionally, the reduced sensitivity to varying elastic modulus indicates
that second-order effects are now a smaller factor, and that the wall operates well within its uncracked
domain. This implies that increasing the wall thickness not only enhances structural capacity, but
also helps to suppress damage evolution (cracking/crushing), especially in larger normal force areas.
Overall, the improved performance of the 216 mm wall supports the hypothesis that part of the
instability observed previously was due to slenderness. A stiffer, thicker wall shows reduced deflections,
improved numerical convergence, and increased resistance to both cracking and second-order deflection
effects.
4.2
Finite Element Method
Linear vs Non-linear Behaviour:
From the simulations, it is seen that the linear model can provide a reasonable estimate for overall
deflection, particularly in the thicker 216 mm walls where the stress state remains within the elastic
domain. For the thinner 108 mm walls, however, the comparison in Figure 3.46 and Figure 3.47 clearly
shows that the linear assumption underestimates deflections compared to the non-linear model. The
non-linear and damaged models, which allow for yielding and cracking, reveal increased deformations,
due to softening, that can’t be captured in a purely linear-elastic approach.
Sensitivity to Material and Geometry:
The influence of stiffness on deflections is evident across all model types, but most pronounced in
the non-linear simulations. As shown in Table 3.22, a lower E-modulus results in significantly higher
deflections—up to three times the value between E = 900 MPa and E = 300 MPa. Moreover, dou-
bling the wall thickness (from 108 mm to 216 mm) consistently reduces deflections by an approximate
factor of 6-8, depending on the loading scenario. This confirms that both the material model and the
wall geometry play major roles. Particularly when looking at the 108 mm walls, non-linear behaviour
and cracking effects become essential to include.
Assumption of Poisson’s Ratio:
Several assumptions were made during this project to align the numerical model with the theoretical
model, such as setting Poisson’s ratio = 0. This assumption removes lateral strains, simplifying stress
states and streamlining the comparison with the theoretical model. However, in real materials, Poisson
effects may redistribute stresses and influence local deformations, which could potentially affect both
the deflection and the failure mechanisms of the structure. The specific impact on overall deflections or
failure patterns remains uncertain and would require further investigation or more advanced modelling
to quantify
104
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
==PAGE:109==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
Mesh and Element Sensitivity:
The mesh element type chosen in this study is C3D8R, which is generally good if the mesh is fine
enough. A C3D20R or even a C3D20 can be used, gaining extra nodes for precision and, if dropping
the ”R”, even more precise results but at the expense of higher computational requirement. Reduced
integration elements (”R”) can be prone to hourglassing, especially in non-linear or softening prob-
lems, which can influence how damage and cracks develop if not handled correctly. Mesh sensitivity is
also an issue in damage and cracking simulations, as a finer mesh can cause more localized strains and
cracks, while a coarser mesh may smear out the damage artificially. Further studies must be made to
explore mesh refinement or higher-order elements to verify accuracy in regions with sudden changes
in gradients.
Convergence:
The linear model shows no convergence issues. The non-linear model (CDP), especially the damaged
instance variant, on the other hand requires more careful treatment to reach convergence. A viscosity
parameter of 0.0001 was introduced to stabilise the simulation and reduce iteration failures. Along
with this, a minimum step size of 10−7 is introduced to allow the program to take suﬀiciently small
steps to take into account rapidly changing stiffnesses.
To allow the wall enough time to reach deformation equilibrium, a total step time of 8 is chosen.
Combined with the small increment size, this ensures a secure convergence and smooth material
response. The time step of 8 is chosen through iteration to a point where convergence is achieved
within the time frame.
The inclusion of a fracture energy criterion, in the damaged CDP model introduces convergence issues
during FEM simulations. This is likely due to the numerically small value added in the form of crack-
ing displacement in the tensile softening, which increases the risk of numerical instability. A solution
to this might be to rethink the units ”chosen” for ABAQUS. Changing all ”units” to MPa and mm
would allow the deflection values to become numerically larger. Another reason could be due to how
the fracture energy approach defines energy dissipation during cracking, which could be caused by
coarse meshing. If the mesh is too coarse relative to the softening slope, strain localises excessively,
leading to abrupt stiffness drops and failure to converge. In unreinforced, brittle materials such as
unfired clay, this sensitivity is amplified.
Definition of softening curve:
The softening curve in compression is chosen to keep on following the polynomial curve derived from
the experimental data, in true stress-strain values. As such, the softening curve should not be in-
terpreted as a direct material property but rather as a defined input curve that characterises the
observed reduction in compressive stress post-peak. By continuing along the polynomial trend, the
model ensures continuity and avoids abrupt transitions in stiffness, while remaining consistent with
the overall deformation characteristics observed in the test specimen. If more experimental testing is
done a more precise softening curve can be defined.
Undamaged CDP Assumption:
In the undamaged CDP model, it is assumed that the material can still follow the constitutive tensile
behaviour defined for reinforced concrete, as long as no damage parameters (dc and dt) are included.
This assumption is considered valid in cases where cracking and stiffness degradation are not of inter-
est. In this state, the material can yield and accumulate permanent (plastic) strains, while maintaining
its full initial stiffness in tension.
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
105
==PAGE:110==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
Damage Analysis:
Finite element programs such as ABAQUS usually have a hard time getting around damage propaga-
tion, due to the sudden loss in stiffness. This is due to the highly localized nature of cracking, which
often leads to abrupt changes in the stiffness matrix and makes it diﬀicult for the solver to converge.
When elements lose stiffness rapidly, the load is redistributed sharply, causing numerical instability
and mesh-dependent results. Using methods such as CDP, where crack propagation is modelled by
gradually lowering the stiffness rather than removing it instantly, some results are easier to obtain.
This gradual reduction aids the solver maintain stability and improves convergence, making it possible
to capture the overall load-displacement response. However, this approach can sometimes smear out
the cracks and may not fully represent discrete crack patterns, but it does make the simulation process
more robust in most practical cases.
Cracking, as modelled in the CDP with damage, appears to primarily affect the 108 mm 3rd floor wall.
The tensile damage distribution (Figure 3.49) shows cracking/damage near the bottom where the wall
is fixed, corresponding well with peak moment location as found from equation 3.2. In contrast, the
216 mm walls exhibit no damage due to staying in the elastic region, reinforcing the conclusion that
a thicker wall is less prone to cracking under similar loads.
Interestingly, even though there is tensile damage of nearly 20%, the increase in deflection due to
cracking is minor, as seen in Table 3.23. This suggests that for the given load levels and stiffnesses,
the cracked zones are not critical enough to lead to large increases in deformation. Damage might
play a bigger part if looking into loading and unloading by, for example, changing the wind on the
3rd floor wall between pressure and suction.
4.3
Theoretical model vs FEM Model
The comparison between the theoretical model and FEM-model’s second-order deflections reveals
consistent trends with expected behaviour. Across both thicknesses and floor positions, deflections
decrease as the elastic modulus increases, confirming the inverse relationship between stiffness and
deformation.
In the linear case, FEM results are generally higher than the theoretical predictions. This is especially
evident in the 108 mm ground floor wall, where FEM shows significantly larger deflections.
The
difference can be attributed to higher sensitivity to second-order effects in slender structures, which
is captured better in the FEM model. A reason for the observed differences also lies in the underlying
assumptions of each method. The theoretical model assumes a linear strain distribution across the wall
cross-section (plane sections remain plane), which simplifies the development of second-order effects
and curvature.
When ABAQUS is using NLGEOM, it allows second-order effects to be present.
This function,
in contrast, does not preserve the assumption that plane cross-sections remain plane when large
deformations are reached. This is due to the strains being evaluated numerically at each integration
point, capturing localized deformations and non-linear curvature more accurately. This goes against
the theoretical model which assumes a linear strain distributions. But since the second-order effect is
a major part of the theoretical model also it was chosen that the second-order contribution has to be
included to get a somewhat meaningful validation between the two models. This can therefore be a
potential explanation for the generally larger deflections observed in walls subjected to second-order
effects (Ground Floor walls), due to the breach of the linear strain assumption. When the strain
distribution becomes non-linear, especially under high axial loads and large deflections, the curvature
of the wall increases disproportionately.
106
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
==PAGE:111==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
For thicker (216 mm) walls, the difference between theory and FEM is minimal, often within 5%,
highlighting that increased wall stiffness suppresses geometric non-linearity. Notably, the theoretical
and FEM results align most closely in the 3rd floor scenarios, where loads are lower and slenderness
effects are smaller.
In the non-linear material models, FEM once again yields higher deflections than the theoretical
models. However, convergence issues arise in the slender 108 mm ground floor wall, where ABAQUS
struggles to find equilibrium in the post-yield region, in tension, for low stiffnesses.
This aligns
with physical intuition, that a soft, slender wall exhibiting damage is expected to experience large
deflections, which are further amplified by second-order effects.
Where convergence is achieved, the FEM model predicts larger deflections than the theory, partly due
to the inclusion of plastic strains and damage accumulation, which are not directly accounted for in
the simplified theoretical approach. The 216 mm FEM results closely match theory again, indicating
that the material remains linear-elastic. One thing to note in the difference between the theoretical
model and the FEM model is the post-yield behaviours.
The theoretical model assumes a brittle failure mode, while the finite element model cannot replicate
this behaviour because it cannot capture sudden drops in stress and stiffness. This difference means
that the ABAQUS model operates over a larger strain range, resulting in greater overall deflections.
The deflection shape analysis confirms that both methods predict similar peak positions along the wall
height. This supports the validity of the theoretical curvature assumptions. Differences in magnitude
are of the same reason mentioned above.
One aspect not clearly reflected in the results is the difference between the elastic region of the CDP
model and the fully polynomial theoretical model. Due to differences in the initial slope of the stress-
strain curves, the CDP model is expected to exhibit greater stiffness in the compressive range before
yielding, whereas the theoretical polynomial model is expected to be stiffer in the tensile range before
cracking.
A small conclusion is, that while FEM tends to produce higher deflections, especially in slender, low-
stiffness cases. The FEM-model seems like a great validation tool to the theoretical model especially in
stiffer walls (thicker or with larger E-modulus). The convergence of the two approaches under higher
E-moduli and thickness adds confidence to the use of simplified models for validation of designs.

June, 2025
5
Conclusion
This thesis aimed to evaluate to what extent second-order deflections and load-bearing capacities of
unfired clay walls could be determined using purely analytical methods, for a wall with combined
axial and lateral load. By deriving and implementing closed-form expressions, a consistent theoretical
model was established that allowed for quick estimation of wall behaviour.
Of three investigated models for the stress-strain curve of the material it was found that the linear
stress-strain curve allows the most progress in analytical calculations. The polynomial stress-strain
curve, despite requiring more computational power, is much closer to the data for the stress-strain
curves, thus yielding more precise results. The computational limit for the second-degree polynomial
stress-strain curve occurs, when deciding the second-order effects on a cracked cross-section. The
”exponential” stress-strain curve calculations have proven more computationally demanding, than is
reasonable to pursue further.
It is possible to analytically determine the cross-sectional capacity diagrams, when the material
strengths are the limiting parameters, for both cracked and uncracked cross-sections. Though it is
generally possible to obtain the expression for the second-order curvature distribution along the wall,
for uncracked cross-sections, the wall’s deflection itself requires numerical methods. The same is true
for the cracked cross-section, though the deflections and subsequent second-order moments require
greater computational capability for second-degree polynomial stress-strain curves, than is currently
available.
Discrepancies came to light with slender wall configurations, especially at lower E-moduli for the
walls with pure lateral load. This is where cracking became significant, and the analytical approach
nonetheless remained a strong tool for assessment of capacities and deflections.
Linear and non-linear material models were implemented in ABAQUS, by including a Concrete Dam-
aged Plasticity (CDP) model calibrated with experimental data. The comparison revealed that the
FEM model and the theoretical model can approximate each others behaviour adequately under high
stiffness and moderate loads, particularly when second-order effects are limited. However, significant
divergence was observed in more slender configurations, at lower E-moduli, with large second-order
effects.
Allowing the wall to crack led to very large analytical expressions which became impossible to evaluate
based on the current mathematical programs used. The Finite Element Program had a hard time
finding convergence when evaluating crack propagation.
During this project the importance of material parameters was investigated, to determine the future
focus of material optimisation. The results lead to the conclusion, that the tensile strength has a sig-
nificant effect when considering walls with little to no normal force applied. For the walls with larger
second-order effects, it was seen that the elastic modulus had a major impact determining whether
the wall would stay within capacity. The compressive strength seemed overall not to contribute much
but if maintaining the assumption that the tensile strength is one-tenth of the compressive strength,
then the increase of compressive strength is equally important to tensile strength.
108
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
==PAGE:113==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
6
Future Work
Mathematical Complexity and Computational Feasibility
During this study, it was theoretically possible to derive an analytical expression for the cracked
curvature, along the height of the wall, using a polynomial stress-strain curve. However, the resulting
equation is too large to be handled within Excel due to formula length limitations. Investigations into
finding program with higher mathematical capacity should be done. In other places of the report,
some equations seem unsolvable, at least for Maple. This raises an important question: Are some
of the problems fundamentally unsolvable, or do they simply exceed the available computational
power in tools such as Maple? Further investigation into advanced analytical computation techniques
or mathematical simplification strategies can aid in clarifying this. Additionally, future studies can
explore the use of numerical approximations or semi-analytical methods to overcome these limitations
and make the models useful for practical applications.
Experimental Validation
While this report has focused on theoretical and numerical approaches, it will be highly beneficial
to validate the findings against experimental data. Comparing the predicted behaviour with results
from real-world wall tests will provide greater confidence in the assumptions and modelling approaches
used. Some things to consider are the accuracy of such large tests and the repeatability thereof.
Furthermore, alternative approximations of stress-strain curves could be explored. For example, a
sinus-curve stress-strain relationships may offer analytical advantages while still capturing realistic
material behaviour.
Less Assumptions
The current model is built under a number of simplifying assumptions that reduce realistic behaviour
in favour of analytical clarity. These include:
• Homogenisation: All calculations assume a homogeneous wall (macro-model), whereas in
reality, masonry walls consist of discrete bricks and mortar joints. It will be relevant to investigate
how modelling individual bricks and joints (micro-model) will impact the results. The complexity
will most likely be too diﬀicult for analytical calculations, but it may be interesting to see how far
one can get with little to no numerical aid. By modelling the structure with individual bricks and
mortar joints instead of as a homogeneous material, additional phenomena such as shrinkage,
interface behaviour, and joint interactions must also be considered which can be interesting to
investigate.
• Poisson’s ratio simplification: The theoretical model assumes ν = 0 to simplify the behaviour
to purely uniaxial effects.
Incorporating Poisson’s effects can provide more accurate strain
distributions.
• Constant moment application: Currently, the moment, from eccentric normal force, is ap-
plied as a constant value at the top of the wall. A more realistic approach will be to apply a
varying moment along the height of the wall, consistent with the walls curvature and deflection
shape.
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
109
==PAGE:114==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
N-M Diagram Parametrisation
Another promising direction is to formulate the N-M diagrams using parametric representations. The
initial expressions for normal force and moment depend only on the central strain.
As such, the
central strain may be set as the parameter. The one downside of this is that it bereaves the relation
between critical normal forces and moments as a function. However, this may reduce the mathematical
complexity of the model and allow for easier identification of capacity limits and yield conditions. Such
simplifications can prove useful in analytical modelling and in numerical solutions.
Openings
The current models are based on fully solid walls without any openings. However, real structures
often include windows and door openings, which can have a significant impact on stress distribution
and failure modes.
Future work should investigate how such discontinuities can be incorporated
into both analytical and numerical models and what measures may be necessary to compensate, e.g.
reinforcement.
Disk Action
In this study, the walls are subjected to transverse wind load and eccentric normal force. However,
in real-world scenarios, shear forces due to in-plane loads (disk action) can also play a role in the
structural behaviour. It would be relevant to extend the model to include in-plane shear behaviour
and assess its impact on failure modes and deformation capacity.
Future Work in ABAQUS
The following future developments are suggested for the numerical modelling in ABAQUS:
• Load scenarios: The current load scenarios are relatively moderate, not fully engaging the
materials post-yield cracking/crushing capabilities. Introducing more extreme or varied load
scenarios may reveal further insights into failure behaviour and capacities.
• Dynamic Analysis: Only static analyses have been performed in this project. A dynamic
analysis, possibly involving loading and unloading phases, can be a good use of the CDP model’s
full potential, particularly for cyclic scenarios. This can allow for a great understanding on how
the damage factor influences the stiffness degradation along with its effect on the capacity of the
wall.
• Mesh Optimization: Mesh sensitivity should be explored, particularly in critical stress regions.
Alternative element types or local mesh refinement strategies can be tested to improve accuracy
and convergence stability
• Less Model Assumptions: Many simplifications were made in the ABAQUS model to align it
with the theoretical framework. An interesting path forward will be to construct a highly realistic
numerical model and compare the results directly with the simplified theoretical predictions and
view how large the differences will be, and this, again, can benefit from experimental work for
extra support.
• Micro-Scale Modelling: Just as mentioned for the theoretical model, a micro-scale ABAQUS
model that simulates bricks and mortar joints would allow exploration of local failure mechanisms
and crack paths in much greater detail.

Appendix
Note: Several of the appendices are Maple files (.mw), which require a Maple license to open. However,
Maple Player is a free software that allows you to view Maple files without a license. Maple Player
can be downloaded free of charge from the MapleSoft website (see link below):
https://www.maplesoft.com/products/maple/MaplePlayer/mapleplay-download.aspx
The following is a description of the different appendices in the report.
A Loads
In this Appendix, load calculations are made. This includes wind-, snow-, live- and deadloads, for
both the 108- and 216 mm wall.
B Stress-Strain Curves
Here are the calculations, where the experimental data from the sample walls are transformed into
stress-strain curves. It also includes calculations for the best fit for the theoretical stress-strain curve
models and the material parameters used in the report.
C Deflections and 2.-Order Effects
Here are shown the calculations needed to find the second order deflections and moments, along with
the equations for the effective thickness. The appendix also includes the results of the calculations.
D N-M Diagrams
Here are shown the calculations and corresponding N-M diagrams that illustrate the cross-sectional
stress capacities, both uncracked and cracked, for the stress-strain curves investigated in this report.
E ABAQUS
Here you can find all ABAQUS model files, which can be used to recreate or adapt the simulations.
Additionally, result files from the completed jobs are included, allowing you to view the analysis
outcomes (note: viewing results requires a license).
Lastly, an Excel document is provided that
summarizes the key results from the modelling, which are also used for the graphs in the report.
Selected ABAQUS contour plots have been presented as well
F Long Equations
In here formulas that are to large for the reports setup have been presented.
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls

A
Loads
A.1
Load calculations
In the Excel-file A1_Load_Calculations.xlsx, load calculations are made, based on load combina-
tions. This includes wind-, snow-, live- and deadloads, for both the 108- and 216 mm wall.
114
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
==PAGE:119==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
B
Stress-Strain Curves
B.1
Stress-strain curves and material parameters
In the Excel file, B1_StressStrain.xlsx, the experimental data from the sample walls are trans-
formed into stress-strain curves. It also includes calculations for the best fit for the theoretical stress-
strain curve models and the material parameters used in the report.
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
115
==PAGE:120==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
C
Deflections and 2.-Order Effects
C.1
Linear_Ground floor_108 mm_Uncracked cross-section
In the Maple file, C1_Lin_Gf_108_Uncr.mw, the calculations and results are found for the
ground floor wall of thickness 108 mm, determined for an uncracked cross-section, using a linear
stress-strain curve.
C.2
Linear_Ground floor_216 mm_Uncracked cross-section
In the Maple file, C2_Lin_Gf_216_Uncr.mw, the calculations and results are found for the
ground floor wall of thickness 216 mm, determined for an uncracked cross-section, using a linear
stress-strain curve.
C.3
Linear_3rd Floor_108 mm_Uncracked cross-section
In the Maple file, C3_Lin_3F_108_Uncr.mw, the calculations and results are found for the 3rd
floor wall of thickness 108 mm, determined for an uncracked cross-section, using a linear stress-strain
curve.
C.4
Linear_3rd Floor_216 mm_Uncracked cross-section
In the Maple file, C4_Lin_3F_216_Uncr.mw, the calculations and results are found for the 3rd
floor wall of thickness 216 mm, determined for an uncracked cross-section, using a linear stress-strain
curve.
C.5
Polynomial with Linear tension_Uncracked cross-section
In the Maple file, C5_PolyLin_Uncr.mw, the calculations are found for an uncracked cross-section,
using a polynomial stress-strain curve with linear tension.
C.6
Polynomial_Uncracked cross-section
In the Maple file, C6_Poly_Uncr.mw, the calculations are found for an uncracked cross-section,
using a polynomial stress-strain curve.
C.7
”Exponential” with Linear tension_Uncracked cross-section
In the Maple file, C7_ExpLin_Uncr.mw, the calculations are found for an uncracked cross-section,
using an ”exponential” stress-strain curve with linear tension.
C.8
”Exponential”_Uncracked cross-section
In the Maple file, C8_Exp_Uncr.mw, the calculations are found for an uncracked cross-section,
using a ”exponential” stress-strain curve.
C.9
Linear_Cracked cross-section_Effective thickness
In the Maple file, C9_Lin_Cr_EffThickness.mw, the calculations are found for the effective
thickness of a cracked cross-section, using a linear stress-strain curve.
C.10
Linear_Cracked cross-section
In the Maple file, C10_Lin_Cr.mw, the calculations are found for a cracked cross-section, using a
linear stress-strain curve.
116
Structural Behaviour of Unfired Clay Walls
==PAGE:121==
Mads Dalby Kamper - s203749
Rasmus Asbjørn Høy-Pedersen - s203735
June, 2025
C.11
Polynomial_Cracked cross-section_Effective thickness
In the Maple file, C11_Poly_Cr_EffThickness.mw, the calculations are found for the effective
thickness of a cracked cross-section, using a polynomial stress-strain curve.
C.12
Polynomial_Cracked cross-section
In the Maple file, C12_Poly_Cr.mw, the calculations are found for a cracked cross-section, using
a polynomial stress-strain curve.
C.13
Numerical calculations
In the Excel file, C13_NumericalCalculations.xlsx, the Numerical calculations are performed for
the different walls and stress-strain curves for both cracked and uncracked cross-sections. The green
cells are the values that must be solved numerically. The blue cells are the cells that must be zero after
finding a solution for the green cells. The results of varying the E-moduli or compressive strengths
are calculated by changing the brown cells, then determining the green cells.
C.14
Results
In the Excel file, C14_Results.xlsx, The results are shown for the second-order deflections and
moments are found for the different wall thicknesses, stress-strain curves, and for uncracked and
cracked cross-sections. The results from varying the E-modulus and compressive strength are also
found here.
